Defending the Author's Praise of His Own Work
The commentator defends the author's praise of his own work against the charge of self-praise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the commentator defends the author's self-praise as sincere advice and gratitude for blessings, not prohibited self-glorification.

Core Idea
Not all praise of one's own work is forbidden; intention and context matter.

This passage addresses a common objection: does the author's praise of his own book amount to prohibited self-praise? The commentator offers a two-part defense, clarifying that the intention was sincere advice and that even if taken as praise, it falls under speaking of God's blessings. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Note the structure: first the objection, then the answer split into two levels.
2
Analyze the first level of the answer
The commentator states the author's true intention was sincere advice (النصح) to Muslims, not self-glorification. Understand how intention changes the ruling.
The proof is that the author wanted people to benefit, not to boast.
3
Analyze the second level of the answer
Even if we assume the intention was praise, the commentator argues it is not prohibited but rather a permissible form of speaking about God's blessings (التحدث بنعمة الله). Self-praise is allowed in certain contexts.
The Quranic verse about speaking blessings is cited as support.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إن قلت: إنّ مدح المصنّف كتابه بأنَّه منقح مصفى من الشبه والعقائد الفاسدة خالٍ من الحشو والتطويل ليس فيه إلَّا لُباب العلم وخالصه ، كل ذلك خلاف الأولى وداخل تحت ما نهى الله تعالى عنه بقوله: ﴿فَلَا تُزَكُوا أَنفُسَكُمْ﴾ [النجم: ۳۲]. أجيب عن ذلك : بأنَّه لم يقصد بهذا الكلام تزكية نفسه ولا تزكية كتابه ، وإنما قصد النُّصح للمسلمين بأن يتعاطوا فهمه وحفظه ليكونوا بمنجاة من خطر التقليد، على أَنَّا لو سَلَّمنا أنَّ مقصوده مدح كتابه ومدح نفسه ؛ لا نُسلّم أنه داخل تحت قوله تعالى: ﴿فَلَا تُزَكُوا أَنفُسَكُمْ [ النجم : ۳۲] ، بل هو من قبيل التّحدث بنعمة الله تعالى عليه المأذون فيه بقوله تعالى: ﴿وَأَمَّا بِنِعْمَةِ رَبِّكَ فَحَدِّثْ﴾ [الضحى: ۱۱] ، ومدح المرء نفسه جائز في مواضع. محي.
If you say: Indeed the author's praise of his book as refined, purified from doubts and corrupt beliefs, free from redundancy and prolixity, containing nothing but the core of knowledge and its purest, all that is contrary to what is proper and falls under what Allah forbade in His saying: 'Do not praise yourselves' [53:32]. The answer to that: He did not intend by this statement to praise himself nor to praise his book, but rather he intended sincere advice to Muslims that they should engage in understanding and memorizing it to be safe from the danger of blind imitation. Moreover, even if we concede that his intention was to praise his book and praise himself, we do not concede that it falls under the verse 'Do not praise yourselves'; rather, it is from the category of speaking about the blessing of Allah upon him, which is permitted in His saying: 'And as for the favor of your Lord, report it' [93:11], and a person praising himself is permissible in certain contexts. End.
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer. The objection is that the author's description of his own book as refined and pure amounts to self-praise, which is prohibited in the Quran. The answer refutes this on two levels: first, the author's intention was not self-praise but sincere advice to the community, encouraging them to benefit from the work. Second, even if it were praise, it is not prohibited self-praise but rather an expression of gratitude for God's blessing, as permitted in the Quran, and self-praise is allowed in certain contexts.

Key Terms
تزكية النفس
مدح
نصح
التحدث بنعمة الله

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any praise of one's own work is automatically forbidden self-praise.
Correction: The commentator shows that intention (sincere advice) and context (speaking about blessings) can make such praise permissible.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, what is the primary intention behind the author's praise of his book?
A) To boast about his own knowledge.
B) To encourage Muslims to benefit from the book.
C) To fulfill a requirement of the genre.
D) To criticize other scholars.

Key Takeaway
The permissibility of praising one's own work depends on the intention and context; it can be a form of sincere advice and gratitude.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you share your successes or achievements, do you examine your intention to ensure it is sincere advice for others rather than self-glorification? How can you cultivate that intention starting today?
?
If someone accused you of self-praise, would your heart react with defense or with a genuine desire to clarify your intention? What does this lesson teach you about checking your own motives before responding?
?
Are you willing to let your work—even if praised—be a means of benefit for others, even if it appears as self-praise? What step can you take this week to use your accomplishments to sincerely advise others?

Method of Learning

5 min
Definitions of Acceptance, Benefit, and Reward
Definitions of qabul, naf', thawab and supplication meaning

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the technical definitions of acceptance (qabul), benefit (naf'), and reward (thawab) as used in Islamic theology, and how the author's supplication expresses hope for these from Allah alone.

Core Idea
True hope is only in Allah for acceptance, benefit, and reward in the Hereafter.

In this passage, the commentator unpacks the key terms from the author's supplication. Understanding these definitions is essential because they ground the student's intention in seeking knowledge. The author shows that his hope is directed solely towards Allah, seeking acceptance, benefit, and reward in the afterlife, not worldly gain.

Guided Path
1
Read the definitions of acceptance and benefit
Read the Arabic definitions of qabul (acceptance) and naf' (benefit) carefully. Notice that qabul involves being pleased and not objecting, while naf' is the opposite of harm, bringing ease.
2
Understand the meaning of reward (thawab)
Thawab is defined as a recompense given by Allah's pure grace, not out of obligation. Note the phrase 'without obligation upon Him nor necessity' – this is a key theological point.
3
Grasp the supplication's overall meaning
The author expresses that he hopes only for Allah's acceptance, benefit from the poem, and reward in the Hereafter. This shows a proper spiritual intention.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
دعاء المصنّف لنفسه ولمُتعلّم هذه المنظومة
والقبولُ للشَّيء : الرّضا به، مع ترك الاعتراض على فاعله، وقيل: الإثابة على العمل الصحيح.
(نافعًا) حالٌ مِن الاسم الكريم ، والنَّفْعُ : ضِدُّ الضَّرَّ ، يُطلق على مَا يحصل به رِفقٌ ومعُونةٌ ، وضمير (بهَا) للأُرجوزة ، أو الجوهرة . وقوله: (مُرِيدا) منصوب بنافعا ، وقوله: (فِي الثَّوَابِ) مُتعلِّقٌ بـ ( طَامِعا ) الواقع صفةً لمريد ، أي : راجيا الثوابَ ، وهو: مقدارٌ مِن الجزاء يعلمه الله تعالى تَفضَّلَ بإعطائه لِمَن شَاء مِن عباده، في نظير أعمالهم الحسنة ، بمخض اختياره، من غير إيجاب عليه ولا وجوب، كما يأتي التصريح به في قول المتن: فَإِنْ يُتِبْنَا فَبِمَحْضِ الفَضْلِ *
والمعنى: لا أرجُو في حصول القبول مني للجوهرة أو الأرجوزة إلَّا الله تعالى، حال كونه نافعاً بها مريداً تحصيل ما يحتاج إليه منها، طامعاً راجيا للثّواب، بمعنى أنَّ قلبه متعلق بحصول الثّواب له في المستقبل، أي: في الآخرة، وليس طامعا في حصول الثَّواب في هذه الحياة الدنيا، ومن هذا الكلام تفهم الفرق بين الرجاء والطمع ؛ فإنّ الرَّجاء: هو تعلق القلب بحصول أمرٍ محبوب في المستقبل، ولا يكون إلا في أمر محبوب، والطمع: تعلق القلب بحصول شىء محبوب، إلا أنه قد يستعمل في المستقبل، وقد يستعمل في الحال، والمستعمل في المحبوب والمكروه.
وفي كلام المصنف الإشارة إلى أنّ العمل الله تعالى مع إرادة الثواب منه سبحانه جائز ، وإن كان غيره أكمل ، وذلك أنَّ درجات الإخلاص ثلاث:
الدرجة العليا: وهي أن يعمل العبد الله وحده امتثالا لأمره وقياما بحق عبوديته .
الدرجة الوسطى : وهي أن يعمل العبد طلبًا للثواب ، وهربا من العقاب.
الدرجة الدنيا: وهي أن يعمل طلبا لإكرام الله له في الدُّنيا وسلامته من آفاتها ، وما عدا هذه المراتب الثلاث يكون رياءً ، وهو أيضاً متفاوت محي .
The author's supplication for himself and the student of this poem.
Acceptance (al-qabul) of a thing: being pleased with it, with leaving objection against its doer. And it is said: rewarding (al-ithaba) for the correct action.
(Nafi'an) is a circumstantial accusative from the noble name, and benefit (al-naf') is the opposite of harm (al-dar). It is applied to what brings ease and assistance. And the pronoun (biha) refers to the urjuzah or the jawharah. And his saying: (muridan) is accusative by nafi'an. And his saying: (fi al-thawab) is attached to (tami'an) which is a description of muridan, i.e., hoping for reward (al-thawab), which is an amount of recompense that Allah knows, He bestows it by His grace upon whom He wills of His servants in return for their good deeds, by His pure choice, without obligation (ijab) upon Him nor necessity (wujub), as will be explicitly stated in the text: 'For if He rewards us, it is by pure grace.'
And the meaning: I do not hope for obtaining acceptance from me of the jawharah or the urjuzah except from Allah, while He is benefitting (nafi'an) with it (biha), desiring (muridan) to obtain what is needed from it, hoping (tami'an) for reward. That is, his heart is attached to obtaining reward in the future, i.e., in the Hereafter, and not hoping for reward in this worldly life. From this speech you understand the difference between hope (al-raja') and desire (al-tama'): For hope is the heart's attachment to obtaining a beloved matter in the future, and it is only for a beloved matter. As for desire, it is the heart's attachment to obtaining a beloved matter, but it may be used for the future or the present, and it may be used for the beloved and the disliked.
And in the author's speech is an indication that acting for Allah with the intention of reward from Him is permissible, even if other than it is more complete. For indeed the degrees of sincerity (al-ikhlas) are three:
The highest degree: that the servant acts for Allah alone, in obedience to His command and fulfilling the right of His servitude.
The middle degree: that the servant acts seeking reward and fleeing from punishment.
The lowest degree: that he acts seeking Allah's honoring him in this world and safety from its afflictions. And other than these three ranks is showing off (riya'), and it is also graded.
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the author's (al-Laqqani) supplication from his poem 'Jawharat al-Tawhid.' It begins by defining 'acceptance' (al-qabul) as being pleased with a thing while refraining from objecting to its doer, and also as rewarding correct action. It then explains the grammatical state of the words 'nafi'an' and 'muridan' in the line, noting that 'benefit' (al-naf') is the opposite of harm and refers to what brings ease and assistance. The pronoun 'biha' is said to refer to the urjuzah (the poem itself) or the jawharah (the essence). The commentary clarifies that the author intends to hope only for Allah's acceptance, while Allah is benefitting and desiring the student's learning. The term 'thawab' (reward) is defined as a measure of recompense given by Allah's pure grace without any obligation upon Him. The passage then distinguishes between 'raja'' (hope) and 'tama'' (desire): hope is attachment of the heart to a future beloved matter, while desire can be for future or present, and can involve both beloved and disliked things. The commentary also explains that acting for Allah with the intention of reward is permissible, though not the highest rank of sincerity. It then enumerates three levels of sincerity (ikhlas): highest – acting solely for Allah out of obedience; middle – acting for reward and fear of punishment; lowest – acting for worldly honor and safety. Any action outside these is considered showing off (riya'), which itself has degrees.

Key Terms
قبول
نفع
ثواب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that reward (thawab) is an obligation upon Allah in return for good deeds.
Correction: The passage clarifies that thawab is by Allah's pure grace, without any obligation (ijab) or necessity (wujub) upon Him.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the definition of acceptance (qabul)?
A) Being pleased with a thing and objecting to its doer.
B) Being pleased with a thing and leaving objection against its doer.
C) Rewarding for correct action only.
D) A state of the heart unrelated to action.

Key Takeaway
The author's supplication teaches that a believer's hope for acceptance, benefit, and reward should be directed solely to Allah, seeking these in the Hereafter.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read that Allah bestows reward by pure grace without obligation, does your heart truly accept that you deserve nothing from Him? Or do you still feel entitlement for your good deeds? How can you cultivate the author's hope (tami'an) to be purely for the Hereafter, not for worldly recognition?
?
The author hopes for acceptance from Allah, not people. Are your actions seeking acceptance from others? How would your life change if you truly directed your hope for acceptance only to Allah, leaving objection to His doers?
?
The passage defines acceptance as being pleased with a thing and leaving objection. Are you ever displeased with Allah's decree? How does this definition challenge your reaction to trials, and what step can you take today to practice leaving objection?

Distinguishing Hope (Raja') from Desire (Tama')
Definition and difference between raja' and tama'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the Islamic concepts of hope (raja') and desire (tama') as defined in the commentary.

Core Idea
Hope is for a future beloved matter; desire can be for present or future and even for disliked outcomes.

The commentator draws an important distinction between two closely related yet different states of the heart. Understanding this difference helps refine one's sincerity and prevents mixing worldly desires with spiritual hope.

Guided Path
1
Read the definitions of raja' and tama'
Carefully read the Arabic definitions. Raja' is attachment to a future beloved matter only; tama' is attachment to a beloved matter but can be for present or future.
2
Note the differences in usage
Observe that raja' is exclusively for future and beloved, while tama' can be used for present or future, and even for disliked outcomes.
3
Reflect on practical implications
Consider how this distinction applies to your own intentions: is your hope for Allah's reward pure raja' or mixed with worldly desire?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
دعاء المصنّف لنفسه ولمُتعلّم هذه المنظومة
والقبولُ للشَّيء : الرّضا به، مع ترك الاعتراض على فاعله، وقيل: الإثابة على العمل الصحيح.
(نافعًا) حالٌ مِن الاسم الكريم ، والنَّفْعُ : ضِدُّ الضَّرَّ ، يُطلق على مَا يحصل به رِفقٌ ومعُونةٌ ، وضمير (بهَا) للأُرجوزة ، أو الجوهرة . وقوله: (مُرِيدا) منصوب بنافعا ، وقوله: (فِي الثَّوَابِ) مُتعلِّقٌ بـ ( طَامِعا ) الواقع صفةً لمريد ، أي : راجيا الثوابَ ، وهو: مقدارٌ مِن الجزاء يعلمه الله تعالى تَفضَّلَ بإعطائه لِمَن شَاء مِن عباده، في نظير أعمالهم الحسنة ، بمخض اختياره، من غير إيجاب عليه ولا وجوب، كما يأتي التصريح به في قول المتن: فَإِنْ يُتِبْنَا فَبِمَحْضِ الفَضْلِ *

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is a key difference between raja' and tama'?
A) Raja' is for worldly matters, tama' for the afterlife.
B) Raja' is attachment to a future beloved matter only, while tama' can be for present or future and for both beloved and disliked.
C) Raja' and tama' are synonyms with no difference.
D) Raja' is for the afterlife, tama' is for worldly matters.

Key Takeaway
Hope (raja') is purely for a future beloved goal, while desire (tama') can be for the present or future and may involve disliked things.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a specific worldly desire you have been chasing for immediate gratification. How can you transform that into a hope for a future, eternal reward that is truly beloved to Allah? What is the first step you will take today?
?
The passage shows that desire (tama') can even be for disliked things. In what area of your life are you ‘desiring’ something you know is disliked by Allah? Why do you hold onto that desire, and how can you replace it with a sincere hope for what pleases Him?
?
Hope (raja') is exclusively for a future beloved matter. Does your hope for the afterlife feel real and active, or is it just a vague wish? What concrete action can you take this week to strengthen that hope and let it guide your choices?

The Three Degrees of Sincerity and Warning Against Riya'
Enumeration of three degrees of ikhlas and warning against riya'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three levels of sincerity in action and the danger of showing off (riya') as taught in the commentary.

Core Idea
The highest sincerity is acting solely for Allah; seeking reward is permissible but lower; seeking worldly gain is lowest.

The commentator explains that while acting for reward is permissible, the highest rank is to worship Allah purely out of obedience. This classification helps students evaluate their intentions and avoid the trap of riya' (showing off).

Guided Path
1
Read the three degrees of sincerity
Read the Arabic enumeration of the three levels. Note that they are ordered from highest to lowest.
2
Understand each degree
The highest: acting solely for Allah out of obedience. The middle: seeking reward and fearing punishment. The lowest: seeking worldly honor and safety.
3
Recognize the warning against riya'
Anything outside these three is considered showing off (riya'), which itself has degrees. Understand that riya' is blameworthy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
دعاء المصنّف لنفسه ولمُتعلّم هذه المنظومة
والقبولُ للشَّيء : الرّضا به، مع ترك الاعتراض على فاعله، وقيل: الإثابة على العمل الصحيح.
(نافعًا) حالٌ مِن الاسم الكريم ، والنَّفْعُ : ضِدُّ الضَّرَّ ، يُطلق على مَا يحصل به رِفقٌ ومعُونةٌ ، وضمير (بهَا) للأُرجوزة ، أو الجوهرة . وقوله: (مُرِيدا) منصوب بنافعا ، وقوله: (فِي الثَّوَابِ) مُتعلِّقٌ بـ ( طَامِعا ) الواقع صفةً لمريد ، أي : راجيا الثوابَ ، وهو: مقدارٌ مِن الجزاء يعلمه الله تعالى تَفضَّلَ بإعطائه لِمَن شَاء مِن عباده، في نظير أعمالهم الحسنة ، بمخض اختياره، من غير إيجاب عليه ولا وجوب، كما يأتي التصريح به في قول المتن: فَإِنْ يُتِبْنَا فَبِمَحْضِ الفَضْلِ *
والمعنى: لا أرجُو في حصول القبول مني للجوهرة أو الأرجوزة إلَّا الله تعالى، حال كونه نافعاً بها مريداً تحصيل ما يحتاج إليه منها، طامعاً راجيا للثّواب، بمعنى أنَّ قلبه متعلق بحصول الثّواب له في المستقبل، أي: في الآخرة، وليس طامعا في حصول الثَّواب في هذه الحياة الدنيا، ومن هذا الكلام تفهم الفرق بين الرجاء والطمع ؛ فإنّ الرَّجاء: هو تعلق القلب بحصول أمرٍ محبوب في المستقبل، ولا يكون إلا في أمر محبوب، والطمع: تعلق القلب بحصول شىء محبوب، إلا أنه قد يستعمل في المستقبل، وقد يستعمل في الحال، والمستعمل في المحبوب والمكروه.
وفي كلام المصنف الإشارة إلى أنّ العمل الله تعالى مع إرادة الثواب منه سبحانه جائز ، وإن كان غيره أكمل ، وذلك أنَّ درجات الإخلاص ثلاث:
الدرجة العليا: وهي أن يعمل العبد الله وحده امتثالا لأمره وقياما بحق عبوديته .
الدرجة الوسطى : وهي أن يعمل العبد طلبًا للثواب ، وهربا من العقاب.
الدرجة الدنيا: وهي أن يعمل طلبا لإكرام الله له في الدُّنيا وسلامته من آفاتها ، وما عدا هذه المراتب الثلاث يكون رياءً ، وهو أيضاً متفاوت محي .
The author's supplication for himself and the student of this poem.
Acceptance (al-qabul) of a thing: being pleased with it, with leaving objection against its doer. And it is said: rewarding (al-ithaba) for the correct action.
(Nafi'an) is a circumstantial accusative from the noble name, and benefit (al-naf') is the opposite of harm (al-dar). It is applied to what brings ease and assistance. And the pronoun (biha) refers to the urjuzah or the jawharah. And his saying: (muridan) is accusative by nafi'an. And his saying: (fi al-thawab) is attached to (tami'an) which is a description of muridan, i.e., hoping for reward (al-thawab), which is an amount of recompense that Allah knows, He bestows it by His grace upon whom He wills of His servants in return for their good deeds, by His pure choice, without obligation (ijab) upon Him nor necessity (wujub), as will be explicitly stated in the text: 'For if He rewards us, it is by pure grace.'
And the meaning: I do not hope for obtaining acceptance from me of the jawharah or the urjuzah except from Allah, while He is benefitting (nafi'an) with it (biha), desiring (muridan) to obtain what is needed from it, hoping (tami'an) for reward. That is, his heart is attached to obtaining reward in the future, i.e., in the Hereafter, and not hoping for reward in this worldly life. From this speech you understand the difference between hope (al-raja') and desire (al-tama'): For hope is the heart's attachment to obtaining a beloved matter in the future, and it is only for a beloved matter. As for desire, it is the heart's attachment to obtaining a beloved matter, but it may be used for the future or the present, and it may be used for the beloved and the disliked.
And in the author's speech is an indication that acting for Allah with the intention of reward from Him is permissible, even if other than it is more complete. For indeed the degrees of sincerity (al-ikhlas) are three:
The highest degree: that the servant acts for Allah alone, in obedience to His command and fulfilling the right of His servitude.
The middle degree: that the servant acts seeking reward and fleeing from punishment.
The lowest degree: that he acts seeking Allah's honoring him in this world and safety from its afflictions. And other than these three ranks is showing off (riya'), and it is also graded.
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the author's (al-Laqqani) supplication from his poem 'Jawharat al-Tawhid.' It begins by defining 'acceptance' (al-qabul) as being pleased with a thing while refraining from objecting to its doer, and also as rewarding correct action. It then explains the grammatical state of the words 'nafi'an' and 'muridan' in the line, noting that 'benefit' (al-naf') is the opposite of harm and refers to what brings ease and assistance. The pronoun 'biha' is said to refer to the urjuzah (the poem itself) or the jawharah (the essence). The commentary clarifies that the author intends to hope only for Allah's acceptance, while Allah is benefitting and desiring the student's learning. The term 'thawab' (reward) is defined as a measure of recompense given by Allah's pure grace without any obligation upon Him. The passage then distinguishes between 'raja'' (hope) and 'tama'' (desire): hope is attachment of the heart to a future beloved matter, while desire can be for future or present, and can involve both beloved and disliked things. The commentary also explains that acting for Allah with the intention of reward is permissible, though not the highest rank of sincerity. It then enumerates three levels of sincerity (ikhlas): highest – acting solely for Allah out of obedience; middle – acting for reward and fear of punishment; lowest – acting for worldly honor and safety. Any action outside these is considered showing off (riya'), which itself has degrees.

Key Terms
إخلاص
رياء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that seeking reward from Allah is a sign of low sincerity and impermissible.
Correction: The passage clarifies that seeking reward is permissible and constitutes the middle degree of sincerity, though the highest is to act purely for Allah.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the highest degree of sincerity (ikhlas)?
A) Acting for Allah to seek reward and avoid punishment.
B) Acting for Allah alone out of obedience and fulfilling servitude.
C) Acting for Allah seeking worldly honor.
D) Acting for Allah with the intention of showing off.

Key Takeaway
There are three levels of sincerity: highest is pure obedience, middle is seeking reward, lowest is seeking worldly gain; all else is riya'.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Which of the three degrees of sincerity do you honestly find yourself in most often when you pray, give charity, or help others? Are you willing to ask Allah to elevate your intention to the highest degree, even if it means giving up any hope of reward or worldly benefit?
?
What worldly gain or safety from affliction are you secretly hoping for when you perform acts of worship? How would your relationship with Allah change if you stopped expecting anything from Him in this world and only sought His pleasure?
?
Is there any act you do that falls outside the three ranks described – that is, done purely for show or to gain status? If so, are you ready to repent and redirect that intention to Allah alone, knowing that riya' is a serious spiritual disease?

The Three Degrees of Sincerity in Intention
Commentator explains the author's prayer and enumerates three degrees of ikhlas.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three degrees of sincerity (ikhlas) and the distinction between hope (raja') and worldly desire (tama') in Islamic theology.

Core Idea
Sincerity has three levels; seeking reward is valid but lower than pure obedience.

Before diving into theological arguments, the author pauses to set his intention. In this passage, he prays for acceptance and then clarifies the degrees of sincerity. This establishes the spiritual framework for the entire work.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Let the author's prayer and the commentator's explanation sink in before analysis.
Notice the verse line quoted: 'If He grants us, it is by pure favor.'
2
Note the distinction between Raja' and Tama'
The commentator defines raja' as hope for future reward in the afterlife, and tama' as desire for worldly benefit. This is a key nuance for understanding intentions.
3
Identify the three degrees of Ikhlas
Find the three ranks mentioned: highest (pure obedience), middle (seeking reward), lowest (seeking worldly honor). Understand their order and why the highest is most perfect.
Any intention outside these three is considered riya' (ostentation).

دعاء المصنّف لنفسه ولمُتعلّم هذه المنظومة
يعلمه الله تعالى تَفضَّلَ بإعطائه لِمَن شَاء مِن عباده، في نظير أعمالهم الحسنة ، بمخض اختياره، من غير إيجاب عليه ولا وجوب وجوب(١)، كما يأتي التصريح به في قول المتن:
=
فَإِنْ يُتِبْنَا فَبِمَحْضِ الفَضْلِ *
في
والمعنى: لا أرجُو في حصول القبول مني للجوهرة أو الأرجوزة إلَّا الله تعالى، حال كونه نافعاً بها (٢) مريداً تحصيل ما يحتاج إليه منها، طامعاً راجيا للثّواب، بمعنى أنَّ قلبه متعلق بحصول الثّواب له في المستقبل، أي: في الآخرة، وليس طامعا في حصول الثَّواب في هذه الحياة الدنيا، ومن هذا الكلام تفهم الفرق بين الرجاء والطمع ; فإنّ الرَّجاء: هو تعلق القلب بحصول أمرٍ محبوب في المستقبل، وفي كلام المصنف الإشارة إلى أنّ العمل الله تعالى مع إرادة الثواب منه سبحانه جائز ، وإن كان غيره أكمل ، وذلك أنَّ درجات الإخلاص ثلاث:
الدرجة العليا: وهي أن يعمل العبد الله امتثالا لأمره وقياما بحق عبوديته .
والدرجة الوسطى : وهي أن يعمل العبد طلبًا للثواب ، وهربا من العقاب.
والدرجة الدنيا: وهي أن يعمل طلبا لإكرام الله له في الدُّنيا وسلامته من آفاتها ، وما عدا هذه المراتب الثلاث يكون رياءً ، وهو أيضاً متفاوت . محي
The author's prayer for himself and for the student of this poem. May Allah Almighty bestow upon it as a favor to whomever He wills from His servants, in return for their good deeds, by pure choice, without any obligation upon Him nor any necessity (1), as will be explicitly stated in the verse of the text: 'If He grants us, it is by pure favor.' And the meaning: I hope for acceptance from me of the Jewel (al-Jawhara) or the poem only from Allah Almighty, while He is beneficent thereby (2), desiring to obtain what is needed from it, hopeful and longing for reward, meaning that his heart is attached to obtaining reward in the future, i.e., in the Hereafter, and not hoping for reward in this worldly life. From this speech you understand the difference between hope (raja') and greed (tama'): indeed hope (raja') is the attachment of the heart to obtaining a beloved matter in the future. And in the author's speech is an indication that performing deeds for Allah with the intention of seeking reward from Him—may He be glorified—is permissible, even if other than that is more perfect. And that is because the degrees of sincerity (ikhlas) are three: The highest degree: that the servant performs deeds for Allah in obedience to His command and standing up to His right of servitude. The middle degree: that the servant performs deeds seeking reward and fleeing from punishment. The lowest degree: that he performs deeds seeking Allah's honoring him in this world and safety from its afflictions. And other than these three ranks is ostentation (riya'), which is also graded. - Muhyi
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This passage defines and explains the three degrees of sincerity (ikhlas) in intention. The commentator first interprets the author's prayerful line, emphasizing that the author hopes only for Allah's favor for the acceptance of his poem, distinguishing between true hope (raja') for the afterlife and worldly greed (tama'). He then states that performing deeds with the intention of seeking reward is permissible, though a lower level of sincerity. The three levels are enumerated: highest: pure obedience to Allah; middle: seeking reward and avoiding punishment; lowest: seeking worldly benefit. Any intention outside these is ostentation (riya'), which itself is graded. The commentary follows the author's own order: first the explanation of the prayer, then the classification of degrees.

Key Terms
إخلاص (ikhlas)
رجاء (raja')
طمع (tama')
درجات الإخلاص (degrees of sincerity)
رياء (riya')

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that seeking reward invalidates sincerity.
Correction: The passage clarifies that seeking reward is a lower degree of sincerity but still valid and permissible.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what distinguishes raja' (hope) from tama' (greed)?
A) Raja' is for worldly gain, while tama' is for afterlife.
B) Raja' is hope for future reward (afterlife), while tama' is desire for worldly benefit.
C) Both are identical in meaning.
D) Raja' is forbidden, while tama' is permissible.

Key Takeaway
The highest sincerity is pure obedience to Allah; seeking reward is permissible but lower, and any intention outside the three degrees is ostentation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Which of the three degrees of sincerity most accurately describes your current intention in your daily acts of worship? Are you content with the middle degree, or do you aspire to the highest? What specific change can you make today to shift your intention towards pure obedience?
?
The passage distinguishes between raja' (hope for the afterlife) and tama' (desire for worldly gain). In what area of your life might you be mistaking worldly desire (tama') for spiritual hope (raja')? How will you correct that?
?
This lesson teaches that seeking worldly honor from Allah is the lowest degree of sincerity, not ostentation. How does this encourage you to evaluate your intentions in acts like seeking knowledge or charitable giving? Will you now check your heart before each action?

Definition of Taklif and Its Basic Conditions
Definition of taklif and its three conditions with exceptions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of taklif (legal responsibility) and the three conditions of puberty, sanity, and receipt of the call, as well as the exceptions for children and the insane.

Core Idea
Taklif requires puberty, sanity, and receiving the prophetic message.

The commentator begins by defining taklif and identifying who is obligated to know God. This passage lays out the essential conditions and clarifies that children and the insane are not held responsible.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of taklif and mukallaf
Read the Arabic passage once. Notice the definition: 'Taklif is imposing something burdensome' and the mukallaf is the pubescent, sane person who has received the call.
2
Identify the three conditions
The passage lists three conditions: puberty (baligh), sanity ('aqil), and receipt of the call (da'wa). Note that these are prerequisites for moral responsibility.
The fourth condition of sound senses is mentioned in a footnote.
3
Examine the exceptions: children and the insane
The commentator explains that children who die before puberty are saved, and the insane are not responsible. Also discusses the Hanafi view on children with intellect.

وجوب معرفة الله على المكلف
الثَّواب منه تعالى بذلك التحصيل ، لا لرياء ولا غيره (۱)
·
وجوبُ معرفة الله على المكلف، وحكم من لم تبلغه الدعوة]
فَكُلُّ مَنْ كُلِّفَ شَرْعًا وَجَبَا عَلَيْهِ أَنْ يَعْرِفَ مَا قَدْ وَجَبَا ١٠ للهِ والْجَائِزَ والمُمْتَنعَا وَمِثْل ذَا لِرُسُلِهِ فَاسْتَمِعا
فَكُلُّ مَنْ كُلِّف) مِن الثَّقلين (۲) ، والتَّكليف : إلزام ما فيه كُلفة (۳)
والمُكلَّف: هو البالغ العاقل الذي بلغته (٤) الدعوة ، فمن لم تبلغه
الدعوة (۱) لا يجب عليه ما ذُكر على الأصح ، ولا يُعذِّبُ (۲) ،
:
وخرج بالبالغ: الصبي فليس الصبي مكلفا ؛ فمن مات قبل البلوغ فهو ناج، ولو كان من أولاد الكفار ، ولا يعاقب على كفر ولا غيره، ونُقل عن الحنفيَّة أنهم قالوا: الصبي الذي يعقل مكلف بالإيمان ، فإن اعتقد الإيمان أو الكفر فأمره ظاهر ، وإن لم يعتقد واحدا منهما كان من أهل النار ، لوجوب الإيمان عليه بمجرد العقل. وخرج بالعاقل المجنون فليس بمكلف، ومثله السكران غير المتعدي بسكره، فإن تعدى بسكره كأن تعمد شرب المسكر ، وهو عالم بحاله فهو كالعاقل ، ومحل هذا إذا بلغ مجنونا أو سكرانَ واستمرَّ على ذلك حتّى مات، فإن بلغ عاقلاً ثم جُنَّ أو سكر متعديا ثم مات فهو على ما كان عليه قبل الجنون والسكر. وخرج بمن بلغته الدعوة: مَنْ لم تبلغه ، وذلك بأن نشأ في شاهق جبل ؛ فليس هذا بمكلف على الأصح، وقيل: هو مكلف لوجود العقل الكافي في وجوب المعرفة، وإن لم تبلغه الدعوة، والذين اشترطوا بلوغ دعوة الرسول يختلفون في أنه: هل الشَّرط أن تبلغه دعوة رسول أي رسول كان ، أم الشَّرط أن تبلغه دعوة الرسول الذي أُرسل إلى أهل زمانه ؟ وذهب جماعة إلى الأوَّل، وذهب جماعة إلى الثَّاني ، وهو مذهب أهل التحقيق وعليه يكون أهل الفترة - وهم الذين كانوا بين أزمنة الرُّسل - ناجين وإن بدلوا وغيَّروا ، وعبدوا الأصنام، والدليل على ذلك قوله تعالى: ﴿وَمَا كُنَّا مُعَذِّبِينَ حَتَّى نَبْعَثَ رَسُولًا ﴾ [الإسراء: ١٥]

(۱) قوله : (لا لرياء ولا غيره) ساقط من المطبوع .
(۲) المراد بالثقلين الإنس والجن، دون الملائكة ، أمَّا على مذهب من قال: (إن الملائكة غير المكلفين أصلا فالأمر ظاهر ، وأما على مذهب من قال: «إن الملائكة مكلفون» ؛ فلأن قائل هذا إنما أراد أنهم مكلفون بالنسبة إلى غير معرفة الله ؛ لأن معرفة الله تعالى جبلية فيهم فليس فيهم من يجهل صفاته تعالى كما في الإنس والجن، ألا ترى إلى قوله سبحانه: شَهِدَ اللَّهُ أَنَّهُ لَا إِلَهَ إِلَّا هُوَ وَالْمَلَتَبِكَةُ وَأُولُوا الْعِلْمِ . محي.
(۳) هذا أحد تعريفين للتكليف والثَّاني أنه طلب ما فيه كلفة . فإنْ فَسَّرته بما فسره به الشارح شمل نوعين فقط ، وهما: الوجوب والتحريم ؛ لأنَّ في الواجب إلزام فعل، وفي الحرام إلزام ترك ، وإنْ فَسَّرْتَه بالثَّاني الذي ذكرناه شمل هذين والندب والكراهة، وذلك لأن الطلب إما أن يكون طلب فعل ، وإما أن يكون طلب ترك ، وكلّ واحدٍ من هذين إما أن يكون جازما ، وإما أن يكون غير جازم، فإن كان طلب فعل جازمًا فهو الوجوب ، وإن كان طلب فعل غير جازم فهو الندب ، وإن كان طلب ترك جازماً فهو التَّحريم ، وإن كان طلب ترك غير جازم فهو الكراهة . محي.
(٤) ذكر الشَّارحُ ثلاثة شروط للتكليف، وهي: البلوغ، والعقل، وبلوغ دعوة الرسول، وتَرَكَ رابعا ، وهو سلامة الحواس ؛ مَع أنه ذكر مُحترزَه في كلام الحافظ ابن حجر ، وهذه الشروط معتبرة في تكليف الإنس؛ فأمَّا الجن فإنَّهم مكلفون من أصل الخلقة ؛ فلا يتوقف تكليفهم على البلوغ.

(۱) قوله: (الذي بلغته الدعوة ولا بد على التحقيق من أن يكون الرسول لهم كما نقله الملوي عن الأبي في شرح مسلم خلافا للنووي فالعرب القدماء الذين أدركوا عيسى من أهل الفترة على المُعتمد لأنه لم يرسل لهم ، وإنما أرسل لبني إسرائيل، وكذا يعطي حكم أهل الفترة من بني إسرائيل من لم يدرك نبينا ونشأ بعد تغيير الإنجيل بحيث لم يبلغه الشرع الصحيح، لا إن بلغه ولو بعد رفع عيسى ، بناء على أن شرع الأنبياء السابقين لا ينسخ إلا بمجيء نبي آخر لا بمجرد الموت الأمير.
(۲) قوله: (ولا يعذب... الخ) أي: لأن الله تعالى وإن كان لا يسأل عما يفعل .. يفعل في ملكه ما يشاء، لكن بمقتضى سبق رحمته لا يقع منه ما تحتار فيه العقول كل الحيرة، فضلا منه تعالى ، ويرحم الله البوصيري حيث يقول:
•
محي
لَمْ يَمْتَحِنَّا بِمَا تَعْيَا العُقول به حِرْصًا علينا فَلَمْ نَرْتَبْ وَلَمْ نَهِم. الأمير وعند الشنواني: قوله: (لا يعذب ويدخل الجنة لأنّ الله تعالى إن كان لا يسأل عما يفعل=
The obligation of knowing God upon the morally responsible. The reward from Him, the Exalted, is for that acquisition, not for show or anything else (1). The obligation of knowing God upon the morally responsible, and the ruling on one whom the call has not reached. So everyone who is legally obligated, it is necessary for him to know what is necessary for God, the possible, and the impossible, and likewise for His messengers, so listen. So everyone who is obligated (from the two heavy burdens (2)) - and legal obligation is imposing what involves difficulty (3) - and the morally responsible is the one who is pubescent, sane, and whom the call has reached (4). So whoever the call has not reached (1), what is mentioned is not obligatory upon him according to the most correct opinion, and he will not be punished (2). And excluded by 'pubescent' is the child: the child is not morally responsible; so whoever dies before puberty is saved, even if he was among the children of the disbelievers, and he is not punished for disbelief or anything else. And it is reported from the Hanafis that they said: The child who has intellect is obligated to believe; if he believes or disbelieves, his case is clear, and if he does not believe in either, he is among the people of the Fire, due to the obligation of faith upon him merely by intellect. And excluded by 'sane' is the insane, so he is not morally responsible, and likewise the drunkard who does not transgress through his drunkenness; if he transgresses through his drunkenness, like deliberately drinking an intoxicant while aware of his state, then he is like the sane. And the place of this is if he reached puberty insane or drunk and continued until death; if he reached puberty sane then became insane or became drunk through transgression, then he is upon what he was before insanity or drunkenness. And excluded by 'the one whom the call has reached' is the one who has not reached it, such as one who grew up on a high mountain; so this one is not morally responsible according to the most correct opinion, and it is said: he is responsible due to the existence of sufficient intellect for the obligation of knowledge, even if the call has not reached him. And those who stipulate the arrival of the call of the messenger differ: is the condition that the call of any messenger reaches him, or that the call of the messenger sent to the people of his time reaches him? And a group went with the first, and a group went with the second, which is the madhhab of the people of verification. According to it, the people of the interval - those who were between the times of the messengers - are saved even if they changed and altered and worshipped idols. The evidence for that is His statement, Exalted: 'And We were not to punish until We send a messenger' [Al-Isra: 15]. (1) His statement: 'not for show or anything else' is omitted from the printed edition. (2) What is meant by the two heavy burdens is humans and jinn, not angels. As for the view of those who say that the angels are not obligated at all, the matter is clear. As for those who say that the angels are obligated, this is because they mean they are obligated with respect to other than knowledge of God; for knowledge of God, Exalted, is innate in them, so none of them is ignorant of His attributes as is the case with humans and jinn. Do you not see His statement, Glorified: 'God bears witness that there is no god but Him, and the angels, and those of knowledge'? - Muhyi. (3) This is one of two definitions of legal obligation; the second is that it is a request for something involving difficulty. If you interpret it as the commentator did, it covers only two types: obligation and prohibition, because in the obligatory there is imposition of an act, and in the forbidden there is imposition of abandonment. If you interpret it by the second one we mentioned, it covers these two and recommendation and dislike, because the request is either a request for an act or a request for abandonment, and each of these is either decisive or non-decisive. If it is a decisive request for an act, it is obligation; if it is a non-decisive request for an act, it is recommendation; if it is a decisive request for abandonment, it is prohibition; if it is a non-decisive request for abandonment, it is dislike. - Muhyi. (4) The commentator mentioned three conditions for legal obligation: puberty, sanity, and receipt of the messenger's call; and he left a fourth, which is soundness of the senses, though he mentioned its exclusion in the words of al-Hafiz Ibn Hajar. These conditions are considered for the obligation of humans; as for the jinn, they are obligated from the origin of creation, so their obligation is not dependent on puberty. (1) His statement: 'whom the call has reached' - and it is necessary, upon verification, that the messenger be for them, as al-Mallawi transmitted from al-Abi in the commentary of Sahih Muslim, contrary to al-Nawawi. So the ancient Arabs who encountered Jesus are among the people of the interval according to the relied-upon view, because he was not sent to them, but was sent only to the Children of Israel. Likewise, the ruling of the people of the interval applies to those of the Children of Israel who did not encounter our Prophet and grew up after the alteration of the Gospel such that the correct law did not reach them, not if it reached him even after the raising of Jesus, based on the fact that the laws of the previous prophets are not abrogated except by the coming of another prophet, not merely by death. - al-Amir. (2) His statement: 'and he will not be punished...' i.e., because God, Exalted, although He is not asked about what He does... He does in His kingdom what He wills, but by virtue of the precedence of His mercy, nothing occurs from Him that confuses the minds completely, as a favor from Him, Exalted. And may God have mercy on al-Busiri where he says: • Muhyi: He did not test us with what the minds are completely perplexed by, out of care for us, so we did not doubt nor did we become confused. - al-Amir. And according to al-Shanwani: His statement: 'He will not be punished and will enter Paradise because God, Exalted, although He is not asked about what He does='
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This passage is a definition and explanation of the concept of taklif (moral responsibility) and its conditions, specifically in relation to the obligation of knowing God. The commentator begins by stating the title: 'The obligation of knowing God upon the morally responsible.' Then he quotes a line of poetry from the matn (Jawharat al-Tawhid) which summarizes the obligation: everyone who is legally obligated must know what is necessary, possible, and impossible for God, and similarly for His messengers. The commentary then defines key terms: 'taklif' is imposing something burdensome, and the 'mukallaf' (legally responsible person) must be pubescent (baligh), sane (aqil), and have received the prophetic call (da'wa). The commentator notes that the most correct view is that if the call has not reached someone, they are not obligated. He then elaborates on the exceptions: children (sabi) are not obligated unless they have intellectual capacity according to some Hanafis; the insane (majnun) and non-transgressive drunkard are not obligated; and those who have not received the call (like someone living on a remote mountain) are not obligated on the most correct opinion. There is a discussion about the condition of the call: whether it must be from any messenger or specifically the messenger sent to that time. The commentator favors the second view, which makes the people of the interval (ahl al-fatrah) saved even if they worshipped idols. The proof is Quran 17:15. The footnotes provide additional details: the two heavy burdens exclude angels because their knowledge of God is innate; the two definitions of taklif cover different types of legal rulings (obligation, prohibition, recommendation, dislike); the three conditions for humans (with a fourth omitted: sound senses); and further clarification on the condition of the call regarding the people of the interval and the fate of those who did not receive the correct message. The last footnote discusses divine justice and mercy, citing al-Busiri's poem.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many assume that every human is automatically responsible before God regardless of age or mental state.
Correction: The passage clarifies that responsibility is contingent upon reaching puberty, being sane, and receiving the prophetic message. Children and the insane are exempt.

Checkpoint

A) Being an adult
B) Being a male
C) Being wealthy
D) Being a scholar

Key Takeaway
Moral responsibility (taklif) requires puberty, sanity, and receipt of the prophetic call; children and the insane are exempt.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever used your sanity, which is a condition for accountability, to deny what you know is true? What would it mean for you to be held accountable for that denial?
?
If you were to die tonight, would you be certain that you have truly fulfilled the obligation of knowing God, not just intellectually, but with a certainty that transforms your life? What evidence would you offer?
?
The passage says the call must reach you. Have you ever ignored or avoided the full weight of the prophetic message, hiding behind excuses? What would it take for you to respond fully today?

Method of Learning
beginner
5 min
The Condition of the Prophetic Call and the People of the Interval
Discussion on the condition of the call and ahl al-fatrah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the role of the prophetic call in moral responsibility and the ruling on those who never received any message, known as the people of the interval (ahl al-fatrah).

Core Idea
Those never reached by a prophetic call are not punished for disbelief.

This lesson focuses on the third condition: receipt of the prophetic call. The commentator discusses different opinions on what constitutes a valid call and concludes that the people of the interval are saved based on the Quranic verse.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the condition of the call
Read the Arabic passage that starts with 'وخرج بمن بلغته الدعوة' and see how the commentator defines who has not been reached by the call.
Note the example of someone raised on a high mountain.
2
Understand the two scholarly opinions
The passage presents two opinions: one that any messenger suffices, and another that the specific messenger to one's time must reach. The commentator favors the second.
This difference affects the status of the people of the interval.
3
Learn about the people of the interval (ahl al-fatrah)
The commentator explains that according to the preferred view, those between messengers are saved, even if they worshipped idols. The evidence is Quran 17:15.

وجوب معرفة الله على المكلف
الثَّواب منه تعالى بذلك التحصيل ، لا لرياء ولا غيره (۱)
·
وجوبُ معرفة الله على المكلف، وحكم من لم تبلغه الدعوة]
فَكُلُّ مَنْ كُلِّفَ شَرْعًا وَجَبَا عَلَيْهِ أَنْ يَعْرِفَ مَا قَدْ وَجَبَا ١٠ للهِ والْجَائِزَ والمُمْتَنعَا وَمِثْل ذَا لِرُسُلِهِ فَاسْتَمِعا
فَكُلُّ مَنْ كُلِّف) مِن الثَّقلين (۲) ، والتَّكليف : إلزام ما فيه كُلفة (۳)
والمُكلَّف: هو البالغ العاقل الذي بلغته (٤) الدعوة ، فمن لم تبلغه
الدعوة (۱) لا يجب عليه ما ذُكر على الأصح ، ولا يُعذِّبُ (۲) ،
:
وخرج بالبالغ: الصبي فليس الصبي مكلفا ؛ فمن مات قبل البلوغ فهو ناج، ولو كان من أولاد الكفار ، ولا يعاقب على كفر ولا غيره، ونُقل عن الحنفيَّة أنهم قالوا: الصبي الذي يعقل مكلف بالإيمان ، فإن اعتقد الإيمان أو الكفر فأمره ظاهر ، وإن لم يعتقد واحدا منهما كان من أهل النار ، لوجوب الإيمان عليه بمجرد العقل. وخرج بالعاقل المجنون فليس بمكلف، ومثله السكران غير المتعدي بسكره، فإن تعدى بسكره كأن تعمد شرب المسكر ، وهو عالم بحاله فهو كالعاقل ، ومحل هذا إذا بلغ مجنونا أو سكرانَ واستمرَّ على ذلك حتّى مات، فإن بلغ عاقلاً ثم جُنَّ أو سكر متعديا ثم مات فهو على ما كان عليه قبل الجنون والسكر. وخرج بمن بلغته الدعوة: مَنْ لم تبلغه ، وذلك بأن نشأ في شاهق جبل ؛ فليس هذا بمكلف على الأصح، وقيل: هو مكلف لوجود العقل الكافي في وجوب المعرفة، وإن لم تبلغه الدعوة، والذين اشترطوا بلوغ دعوة الرسول يختلفون في أنه: هل الشَّرط أن تبلغه دعوة رسول أي رسول كان ، أم الشَّرط أن تبلغه دعوة الرسول الذي أُرسل إلى أهل زمانه ؟ وذهب جماعة إلى الأوَّل، وذهب جماعة إلى الثَّاني ، وهو مذهب أهل التحقيق وعليه يكون أهل الفترة - وهم الذين كانوا بين أزمنة الرُّسل - ناجين وإن بدلوا وغيَّروا ، وعبدوا الأصنام، والدليل على ذلك قوله تعالى: ﴿وَمَا كُنَّا مُعَذِّبِينَ حَتَّى نَبْعَثَ رَسُولًا ﴾ [الإسراء: ١٥]

(۱) قوله : (لا لرياء ولا غيره) ساقط من المطبوع .
(۲) المراد بالثقلين الإنس والجن، دون الملائكة ، أمَّا على مذهب من قال: (إن الملائكة غير المكلفين أصلا فالأمر ظاهر ، وأما على مذهب من قال: «إن الملائكة مكلفون» ؛ فلأن قائل هذا إنما أراد أنهم مكلفون بالنسبة إلى غير معرفة الله ؛ لأن معرفة الله تعالى جبلية فيهم فليس فيهم من يجهل صفاته تعالى كما في الإنس والجن، ألا ترى إلى قوله سبحانه: شَهِدَ اللَّهُ أَنَّهُ لَا إِلَهَ إِلَّا هُوَ وَالْمَلَتَبِكَةُ وَأُولُوا الْعِلْمِ . محي.
(۳) هذا أحد تعريفين للتكليف والثَّاني أنه طلب ما فيه كلفة . فإنْ فَسَّرته بما فسره به الشارح شمل نوعين فقط ، وهما: الوجوب والتحريم ؛ لأنَّ في الواجب إلزام فعل، وفي الحرام إلزام ترك ، وإنْ فَسَّرْتَه بالثَّاني الذي ذكرناه شمل هذين والندب والكراهة، وذلك لأن الطلب إما أن يكون طلب فعل ، وإما أن يكون طلب ترك ، وكلّ واحدٍ من هذين إما أن يكون جازما ، وإما أن يكون غير جازم، فإن كان طلب فعل جازمًا فهو الوجوب ، وإن كان طلب فعل غير جازم فهو الندب ، وإن كان طلب ترك جازماً فهو التَّحريم ، وإن كان طلب ترك غير جازم فهو الكراهة . محي.
(٤) ذكر الشَّارحُ ثلاثة شروط للتكليف، وهي: البلوغ، والعقل، وبلوغ دعوة الرسول، وتَرَكَ رابعا ، وهو سلامة الحواس ؛ مَع أنه ذكر مُحترزَه في كلام الحافظ ابن حجر ، وهذه الشروط معتبرة في تكليف الإنس؛ فأمَّا الجن فإنَّهم مكلفون من أصل الخلقة ؛ فلا يتوقف تكليفهم على البلوغ.

(۱) قوله: (الذي بلغته الدعوة ولا بد على التحقيق من أن يكون الرسول لهم كما نقله الملوي عن الأبي في شرح مسلم خلافا للنووي فالعرب القدماء الذين أدركوا عيسى من أهل الفترة على المُعتمد لأنه لم يرسل لهم ، وإنما أرسل لبني إسرائيل، وكذا يعطي حكم أهل الفترة من بني إسرائيل من لم يدرك نبينا ونشأ بعد تغيير الإنجيل بحيث لم يبلغه الشرع الصحيح، لا إن بلغه ولو بعد رفع عيسى ، بناء على أن شرع الأنبياء السابقين لا ينسخ إلا بمجيء نبي آخر لا بمجرد الموت الأمير.
(۲) قوله: (ولا يعذب... الخ) أي: لأن الله تعالى وإن كان لا يسأل عما يفعل .. يفعل في ملكه ما يشاء، لكن بمقتضى سبق رحمته لا يقع منه ما تحتار فيه العقول كل الحيرة، فضلا منه تعالى ، ويرحم الله البوصيري حيث يقول:

محي
لَمْ يَمْتَحِنَّا بِمَا تَعْيَا العُقول به حِرْصًا علينا فَلَمْ نَرْتَبْ وَلَمْ نَهِم. الأمير وعند الشنواني: قوله: (لا يعذب ويدخل الجنة لأنّ الله تعالى إن كان لا يسأل عما يفعل=
The obligation of knowing God upon the morally responsible. The reward from Him, the Exalted, is for that acquisition, not for show or anything else (1). The obligation of knowing God upon the morally responsible, and the ruling on one whom the call has not reached. If you interpret it by the second one we mentioned, it covers these two and recommendation and dislike, because the request is either a request for an act or a request for abandonment, and each of these is either decisive or non-decisive. If it is a decisive request for an act, it is obligation; if it is a non-decisive request for an act, it is recommendation; if it is a decisive request for abandonment, it is prohibition; if it is a non-decisive request for abandonment, it is dislike. - Muhyi. Commentary
This passage is a definition and explanation of the concept of taklif (moral responsibility) and its conditions, specifically in relation to the obligation of knowing God. The commentator begins by stating the title: 'The obligation of knowing God upon the morally responsible.' Then he quotes a line of poetry from the matn (Jawharat al-Tawhid) which summarizes the obligation: everyone who is legally obligated must know what is necessary, possible, and impossible for God, and similarly for His messengers. The commentary then defines key terms: 'taklif' is imposing something burdensome, and the 'mukallaf' (legally responsible person) must be pubescent (baligh), sane (aqil), and have received the prophetic call (da'wa). The commentator notes that the most correct view is that if the call has not reached someone, they are not obligated. He then elaborates on the exceptions: children (sabi) are not obligated unless they have intellectual capacity according to some Hanafis; the insane (majnun) and non-transgressive drunkard are not obligated; and those who have not received the call (like someone living on a remote mountain) are not obligated on the most correct opinion. There is a discussion about the condition of the call: whether it must be from any messenger or specifically the messenger sent to that time. The commentator favors the second view, which makes the people of the interval (ahl al-fatrah) saved even if they worshipped idols. The proof is Quran 17:15. The footnotes provide additional details: the two heavy burdens exclude angels because their knowledge of God is innate; the two definitions of taklif cover different types of legal rulings (obligation, prohibition, recommendation, dislike); the three conditions for humans (with a fourth omitted: sound senses); and further clarification on the condition of the call regarding the people of the interval and the fate of those who did not receive the correct message. The last footnote discusses divine justice and mercy, citing al-Busiri's poem.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that anyone who does not believe in God is automatically punished, regardless of whether they received a prophetic message.
Correction: The passage clarifies that those who never received a valid prophetic call (ahl al-fatrah) are not punished, as God does not punish without sending a messenger.

Checkpoint
According to the preferred opinion in the passage, what is the condition for a person to be morally responsible?
A) They must have reached puberty
B) They must have received the call of any messenger
C) They must have received the call of the messenger sent to their time
D) They must be able to reason independently

Key Takeaway
The condition of receiving the prophetic call is crucial; those who never received it (ahl al-fatrah) are not held accountable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have received the call of the final messenger, Muhammad ﷺ. When you sin, do you act as if you are still in ignorance like those of the interval? How can you truly internalize that you are accountable? Commit to one specific change today.
?
The people of the interval were saved despite idol worship because they had no messenger. But you have the Quran and the Sunnah. What knowledge have you possessed but not acted upon? Write down one truth you will start living by from this lesson.
?
This lesson shows that God's justice delays punishment until a messenger is sent. Are you responding to the messengers that have come to you — the Prophet, your teachers, the Quran? If not, what is the real reason you are hesitating? Take one concrete step this week to close that gap.

Obligation to Know God's Attributes in Detail and in General
Explains the obligation to know God's attributes and the types of evidence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between detailed (tafsili) and general (ijmali) knowledge of God's attributes based on the type of evidence available.

Core Idea
Not all divine attributes require detailed knowledge; general evidence suffices for general belief.

This passage from the sharh clarifies the scope of the obligation to know God's attributes. It introduces a key methodological principle: the type of evidence (detailed or general) determines the level of knowledge required. This prevents the burden of knowing every single attribute individually, while still affirming the necessity of belief in all of them.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice how the passage uses the terms 'tafsilan' and 'ijmalan'.
2
Identify the two categories of evidence
Find where the passage mentions 'tafsilan' (in detail) and 'ijmalan' (in general). Note that the twenty attributes are the ones proven in detail.
The phrase 'وهو العشرون صفة الآتي بيانها' is key.
3
Understand the implication for obligation
Ask yourself: what is the consequence of this distinction? The passage explicitly states that the obligation differs accordingly.
This connects to the learning objective: evidence type determines knowledge level.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أنه يجب على المكلف أن يعرف جميع ما وجب له سبحانه، لكن بعض الكمالات التي أن يتصف بها الله تعالى قد قامت الأدلة العقلية أو النقلية عليه تفصيلا ، وهو العشرون صفة الآتي بيانها ، وبعضها قد قامت الأدلة العقلية والنقلية عليه إجمالا ، وكذلك المستحيل حقه ، وعلى ذلك يختلف الواجب، فما قامت الأدلة العقلية أو النقلية عليه تفصيلاً يجب على المكلف أن يعرفه تفصيلا، وما قامت الأدلة العقلية أو النقلية عليه إجمالا وجب على المكلف أن يعرفه إجمالاً .
That it is obligatory upon the responsible person (mukallaf) to know all that is wajib (necessary) for Him, exalted. But some of the perfections with which Allah, exalted, is described, the rational or transmitted evidences have been established for them in detail (tafsilan), and these are the twenty attributes to be explained later. And some of them, the rational or transmitted evidences have been established for them in general (ijmalan). And likewise for the impossible (mustahil) in His right. And according to that, the wajib (obligatory) differs: so whatever the rational or transmitted evidences have been established for it in detail, it is obligatory upon the mukallaf to know it in detail; and whatever the rational or transmitted evidences have been established for it in general, it is obligatory upon the mukallaf to know it in general.
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage is an explanation (sharh) of the word 'يجب' (it is obligatory) from the matn. It clarifies that the obligated person (mukallaf) must know all attributes that are necessary for God. However, some perfections are proven by detailed evidence (tafsili) – these are the twenty attributes that will be explained later – while others are proven by general evidence (ijmali). The same applies to the impossible attributes. Therefore, the obligation varies: what is proven in detail must be known in detail, and what is proven in general must be known in general. This establishes the principle that not all theological knowledge requires the same level of detail; general belief suffices for attributes proven by general evidence.

Key Terms
مكلف
معرفة
دليل عقلي
دليل نقلي
تفصيل
إجمال
صفات عشرون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think every divine attribute must be known in detail individually.
Correction: This passage clarifies that only attributes for which detailed evidence exists require detailed knowledge; others can be believed in general.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how many attributes are proven in detail (tafsilan)?
A) Ten
B) Twenty
C) Thirty
D) Fifty

Key Takeaway
The obligation to know God's attributes varies based on the type of evidence: detailed evidence demands detailed knowledge, while general evidence suffices for general belief.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are you satisfied with knowing Allah's attributes in general for those not explicitly proven in detail, or do you feel a need to investigate every single one? How does this lesson free you from unnecessary burden?
?
In your journey of learning about Allah, where have you demanded detailed proofs for matters that only require general belief? How can you apply the principle of 'general evidence suffices' to find peace?
?
The passage mentions both rational and transmitted evidences. Do you trust rational arguments for divine attributes as much as scriptural ones? What would it take for you to embrace both without hesitation?

Obligation to Know the Rational Judgments and Capacity for Understanding
States that all legally responsible persons must know the rational judgments and discusses the condition of capacity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that all legally responsible persons, including common people, are obligated to know the rational judgments from evidence, and the debate about the condition of capacity.

Core Idea
Everyone with intellect must use evidence for creed, but the level of proof is general.

This passage extends the obligation of knowing the rational judgments to all classes of people. It also addresses a key debate: whether a special 'capacity' (ahliyya) is required to understand the proofs, or whether basic intellect suffices. Imam al-Sanūsī clarifies that the required proof is a general one (dalīl jumlī), accessible to all.

Guided Path
1
Read the main text and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to get the overall meaning. Notice the list of categories included among the legally responsible.
The text says 'provided that they have the capacity' — watch for the condition.
2
Understand the scope of obligation
Identify all groups mentioned: common people, slaves, women, servants. They are all obligated to know beliefs from evidence.
3
Analyze the condition of capacity
The main text says they must have capacity (ahliyya). Then read the footnote from al-Sanūsī who argues that capacity is extremely rare or non-existent, and that basic intellect suffices.
This is a point of contention; understand both sides.
4
Learn about the general proof
The commentator explains that the requirement is a general proof (dalīl jumlī), which is easy for anyone with basic intellect.
General proof is a simple argument, not detailed scholarly analysis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ودخل في المكلَّف: العوام، والعبيد ، والنِّسوان، والخَدَمُ؛ فإنَّهم مكلَّفونَ بمعرفة العقائد عن الأدلة ، متى كان فيهم أهليَّة فهمها (۱) ، وإلا كفاهم التقليد.
(1) قوله: متى كان فيهم أهلية فهمها ردَّ ذلك الإمام السنوسي في «شرح الوسطى» بقوله: إن الأهلية في غاية الندور ، أو ليس بموجود أصلا ، فإن الظاهر أن كل من معه أصل عقل التكليف فهو متمكن من المعرفة والنظر . اهـ :أي: لأن المطلوب الدليل الجملي، وهو متيسر من كل من معه أصل عقل التكليف . الشنواني.
And included among the legally responsible (al-mukallaf) are: the common people, slaves, women, and servants; for they are obligated to know the beliefs from the evidences, provided that they have the capacity to understand them, otherwise imitation (taqlīd) suffices them.
(1) His saying: 'provided that they have the capacity to understand them' — Imam al-Sanūsī refuted that in his 'Sharḥ al-Wusṭā' by saying: 'Capacity is extremely rare, or not existent at all, for the apparent is that everyone who has the basic intellect of legal responsibility is capable of knowledge and reasoning.' End. That is because what is required is the general proof (al-dalīl al-jumlī), which is available to everyone who has the basic intellect of legal responsibility. Al-Shanwānī.
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage extends the obligation to know the rational judgments (defined in the previous lesson) to all classes of legally responsible persons (al-mukallaf): common people, slaves, women, and servants. They are required to know the creedal beliefs based on evidence, provided they have the capacity (ahliyya) to understand the proofs. If they lack capacity, then mere imitation (taqlīd) suffices. The footnote then presents a critical point from Imam al-Sanūsī, who rejects the condition of capacity as understood by some. Al-Sanūsī argues that such capacity is extremely rare or non-existent, and the correct view is that anyone who has the basic intellect that makes them legally responsible is capable of knowledge and reasoning. The commentator (al-Shanwānī) explains that this is because the requirement is a general proof (al-dalīl al-jumlī), which is accessible to every responsible person. Thus, the lesson clarifies both the inclusive scope of the obligation and a key theoretical debate about the nature of that obligation.

Key Terms
المكلَّف (al-mukallaf)
العوام (al-ʿawāmm)
التقليد (al-taqlīd)
الأهلية (al-ahliyya)
الدليل الجملي (al-dalīl al-jumlī)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Common people do not need to use evidence for their faith; mere imitation is enough.
Correction: The passage shows they are obligated to know beliefs from evidence; taqlid only suffices if they lack capacity, but Sanusi argues they all have basic capacity.

Checkpoint
According to Imam al-Sanūsī's position as cited in the footnote, what is the status of the capacity (ahliyya) to understand detailed proofs?
A) It is necessary for all mukallafūn
B) It is extremely rare or non-existent
C) It is only for the common people
D) It is achieved through taqlīd

Key Takeaway
All legally responsible persons, including common people, are required to know their beliefs from evidence, though the level of proof is general and accessible to anyone with basic intellect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you personally examined the evidences for your own creed, or are you merely following what you were taught? What specific evidence do you have for your belief in God and the Prophet? If you cannot articulate it, does that not place you in the category of those who lack capacity? But the lesson says capacity is universal. So what is holding you back from seeking that evidence today?
?
Imam al-Sanūsī argued that every legally responsible person has the capacity to understand the general proof. Do you truly believe you have that capacity? If yes, have you acted on it, or do you remain content with imitation? If no, then you must believe that Allah has obligated you to do something you cannot do. Which position do you take, and what does that say about your understanding of divine justice?
?
The passage distinguishes between those with capacity and those without. Reflect on your own journey: Have you ever made a sincere effort to understand the rational proofs for your faith? If not, why not? What practical step can you take this week to move from taqlid to knowledge?

The Crescent Moon Example: Muqallid vs. Guided Student
Shanwani illustrates the muqallid vs. guided student with the crescent moon analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the crescent moon example illustrates the difference between a muqallid (blind follower) and a student who has grasped the evidence.

Core Idea
Blind acceptance is taqlid; understanding the evidence liberates the mind.

In defining the muqallid, Sanusi adds the crucial condition 'without proof.' This can seem abstract until Shanwani clarifies with a vivid example. The crescent moon story makes the distinction concrete, showing that following with understanding is not taqlid.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Let the example unfold naturally.
Notice how the example is structured around two groups.
2
Identify the two groups
The passage describes two groups looking for the crescent moon. The first group sees it; the second group believes them without seeing. Then the first group guides the second with signs until they see it themselves. Ask: which group corresponds to muqallids?
The key is the presence or absence of direct evidence.
3
Compare their answers
The passage highlights the different responses when asked about the moon. The first group says 'they said it appeared' (relying on others), while the second says 'I saw it with my own eyes' (relying on direct observation). This shows independence from taqlid.
This difference in language reflects the state of knowledge.
4
Apply to the definition of muqallid
Reflect on the original condition 'without proof.' The muqallids in the example had no proof themselves; the guided students were led to the proof (direct sight). So, a student who understands the evidence is not a muqallid.
This clarifies that following a teacher who provides proof does not make one a muqallid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۱) قوله: (من غير حجة خرجت التلامذة بعدَ أنْ يُرشدهم الأشياخ للأدلة ، فهم عارفون بعد، وضرب السنوسي في شرح الجزائرية مثالا للفرق بينهم وبين المقلدين بجماعة نظروا للهلال فسبق بعضهم لرؤيته ؛ فأخبروا الجامعة المسبوقة وصدقوهم من غير معاينة كانوا مقلدين ، وإن أرشدتهم - الجماعةُ الأولى التي سبقت ـ بالعلامات حتى عثرت الثانية أيضا على الهلال وعاينوه استقلوا عن التقليد، ألا ترى أنَّ الأولى إذا سئلت عن الهلال كان جوابها: قالوا إنه ظهر . والثانية تقول: إني رأيته بعيني.
(1) He said: 'Without proof' excludes the students after the teachers guide them to the proofs, for they are knowers after [guidance]. And al-Sanusi gave in his commentary on al-Jazairiyyah an example of the difference between them and the muqallids: a group looked for the new crescent; some preceded in seeing it, so they informed the second group, and the second group believed them without direct sight; those were muqallids. But if the first group guided the second by signs until the second also spotted and directly saw the crescent, they become independent of taqlid. Do you not see that the first group, when asked about the crescent, their answer is: 'they said it appeared.' The second group says: 'I saw it with my own eyes.'
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This is an illustrative example from a footnote. It clarifies the condition in the definition that the muqallid acts 'without proof.' The example distinguishes between students who are guided by teachers to the proofs and thus become knowers, and muqallids who simply accept without seeing the evidence. The crescent moon analogy is used: a group A sees the moon and informs group B, who believe them without seeing; group B are muqallids. If group A then guides group B with signs until group B sees the moon themselves, they are no longer muqallids. The difference in their answers shows the independence.

Key Terms
مقلد
تقليد
دليل
هلال
معاينة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That all followers of scholars are muqallids, even if they understand the evidence.
Correction: The example shows that students who have been guided to the proofs and understand them are not muqallids; only those who accept without direct knowledge are.

Checkpoint
In the crescent moon example, what distinguishes the muqallids from the independent observers?
A) The muqallids were more numerous
B) The muqallids did not see the moon directly but accepted others' testimony
C) The muqallids were the first to see the moon
D) The muqallids used telescopes

Key Takeaway
The crescent moon example shows that a student who has been guided to the evidence is not a muqallid; only those who accept without direct understanding are.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a core belief you hold about Allah or the unseen. Can you express the evidence from revelation or reason that supports it, or do you merely repeat what you've been taught? If you cannot, are you not like the one who says 'they said it appeared'? What specific step will you take this week to move from 'they said' to 'I see'?
?
The example contrasts 'they said it appeared' with 'I saw it with my own eyes.' In your daily life, what difference would it make if you could say the same about your faith? Does your conviction rest on your own reflection or on the authority of others? If your faith were tested, would your answer be as weak as hearsay or as strong as direct sight?
?
Imagine standing before Allah and being asked why you believed in Him. Would your response reflect a muqallid's dependence on others or a guided student's personal certainty? The lesson shows that guidance leads to independence from taqlid. What is one proof of Allah's existence you can articulate right now, and if you cannot, what is your plan to acquire that evidence?
