النَّظرُ في النَّفْسِ والعَالَم بحيث يوصل إلى معرفة الله ذاتكَ (۱) ، لأنَّها أقرب الأشياء إليكَ ؛ لقوله تعالى: ﴿وَفِي أَنفُسِكُمْ أَفَلَا تُبْصِرُونَ﴾ [الذاريات: ۲۱] ﴿وَلَقَدْ خَلَقْنَا الإِنسَانَ مِن سُلَلَةٍ مِّن طِينٍ﴾ [المؤمنون: ۱۲] فَتَستَدل بها. أشار المصنف بذكر وجوب التفكر في أحوال ذاته ؛ لأمور أولها أنها أقرب الأشياء إليه ، وثانيها: أنه قد ورد: «مَن عرف نفسه فقد عرف ربه» أي من تفكر في بدائعها توصل إلى معرفة صانعها، وقيل معناه من عرف نفسه بالحدوث والفقر عرف ربه بالقدم والغنى، ألا أنك لو نظرت إلى أحوال ذاتك القريبة منك علمت أن أحوالها هذه متغيرة ، فأنت تارة معافى وتارة مريض، وأنت تارة واجد ما تريد، وتارة غير واجد لما تريد، وتارة قائم، وتارة قاعد، وتارة نائم، إلى غير ذلك من الأحوال التي تعرض لك ثم تزول، وإذا علمت أنها متغيرة أيقنت أنها حادثة ، وأيقنت مع ذلك أن ذاتك نفسها حادثة لأنها ملازمة لهذه الأمور الحادثة لا يمكن أن تنفك ذات عن بعض هذه الأمور ، وإذا علمت أن ذاتك وأحوالها حادثة أيقنت أن لا بد لها من محدث حكيم واجب الوجود عام العلم تام القدرة والإرادة ؛ فهذا طريق استدلال الإنسان بالنظر والتفكر في أحوال ذاته على وجوب وجود صانع حكيم، وكذلك التفكر في أحوال العالَميْنِ العلوي والسفلي فإنه سيجد كلا منهما مشمولا بجهات مخصوصة وأمكنة معينة، وسيجد بعضها متحركا وبعضها ساكنا، وبعضه نورانيا وبعضه ظلمانيا ، وسيجد أن هذه الأحوال تتغير إلى مقابلاتها، وهذا أمارة الحدوث، وهو أمارة الاحتياج إلى مُحدث، وقد نعى الله تعالى على من ترك الفكر في أمر نفسه بقوله سبحانه: ﴿وَفِي أَنفُسِكُمْ أَفَلَا تُبْصِرُونَ﴾ [الذاريات: ۲۱] يريد سبحانه ـ والله أعلم ـ وفي أنفسكم آيات دالة على حدوثكم وافتقاركم إلى محدث يوجد، متصف بجميع صفات الكمال ، أتتركون التفكر فيها فلا تبصرون. وهذا الاستفهام بمعنى النفي، أي: لا ينبغي لكم ترك النظر في أحوال ذواتكم، ونبّه سبحانه على وجوب التفكر في العالمين العلوي والسفلي بقوله: ﴿إِنَّ فِي خَلْقِ السَّمَوَاتِ وَالْأَرْضِ وَاخْتِلَفِ الَّيْلِ وَالنَّهَارِ وَالْفُلْكِ الَّتِي تَجْرِي فِي الْبَحْرِ بِمَا يَنفَعُ النَّاسَ وَمَا أَنزَلَ اللَّهُ مِنَ السَّمَاءِ مِن مَّاءٍ فَأَحْيَا بِهِ الْأَرْضَ بَعْدَ مَوْتِهَا وَبَثَّ فِيهَا مِن كُلِّ دَابَّةٍ وَتَصْرِيفِ الرِّيحِ وَالسَّحَابِ الْمُسَخَّرِ بَيْنَ السَّمَاءِ وَالْأَرْضِ لَآيَاتٍ لِقَوْمٍ يَعْقِلُونَ﴾ [البقرة: ١٦٤]. (1) قوله: (في أحوال ذاتك) أشار بذلك إلى أنَّ (انظر) بمعنى: تفكر، لأنه الذي يتعدى بمعنى (في) ويحتمل أن تكون (إلى) في قوله : (إلى نفسك) باقية على أصلها، على أنَّ النظر متضمن معنى توجه أو انتبه. وأن المراد بالنظر في الذات التفكر في أحوالها ؛ إذ ليس له معنى إلا هذا ، أو أن المراد بالنفس الذات . الشنواني. النَّظرُ في النَّفس والعالم بحيث يوصل إلى معرفة الله على وجوب وجود صانعك وصفاته، فإنها(۱) مشتملة على سمع، وبصر، وكلام، وطولٍ وعَرض، وعُمق، ورضا، وغضب، وبياض، وحمرة، وسَوادٍ ، وعِلمٍ ، وجهل (۲) ، وإيمان ، وكُفر ، ولذَّةٍ، وألم (۳) ، وغير ذلك مما لا يُحصى، وكلُّها متغيّرة وخارجةٌ مِن العدم إلى الوجود، ومن الوجود إلى العدم، وذلك دليل الحدوثِ والافتقار إلى صانع حكيم واجب الوجود ، عام العلم ، تامّ القدرة والإرادة، فتكون حادثةً، قائمة بالذات لازمة لها، وهي وملازم الحادثِ حادث أيضا (٤). وأشار إلى طريق آخرَ يُوصل النَّظرُ فيه إلى معرفة وجوب وجود الصانع وصفاته بقوله: (۱) قوله: (فإنها ) أي : نفسكَ ، مشتملة تعليل لقوله: تستدل . الأمير . (۲) قوله: (وجهل أي مركب ، وأما البسيط فليس بعرض؛ لأنه عدم، والعرض مخصوص بالوجود. (۳) قوله : (وكُفر) هو عرض إن فُسر بالجحد، وإن فُسر بعدم الإيمان فهو من قبيل العدم. قوله: (ولذة وألم اللذة والألم إدراكان ، فالأول : إدراك ما هو خير له ، والألم: هو إدراك ما هو شر له ، والإدراك عرض لأنه من الأمور الموجودة بتغيير من وجود إلى عدم وبالعكس. الأمير والشنواني. (٤) قوله : ( وملازم الحادث حادث المراد بالمُلازم الذات، وبالحادث الأعراض، والمعنى: الذات الملازمة للأعراض الحادثة حادثة مثلها ، فقد أطلق الملازم على الملزوم ، أي : وملزوم الحادث حادث ؛ إذ الملازمة بين الشيئين أن يكون كل منهما لازما للآخر، وليست ذاتك المخصوصة لازمة لأجناس تلك الصفات ؛ إذ أجناس تلك الصفات توجد في غير نفسك، وما ذكره في قوة مقدمتين تقديرهما هكذا: نفسك ملزومة لصفات حادثة ، وكل ملزوم للصفات الحادثة فهو حادث ، ينتج نفسك حادثة، ودليل الصغرى ما تقدم، ودليل الكبرى: أن ملزوم الحادث لا يسبقه، وإلا لوجد الملزوم بدون اللازم، وإن لم يسبقه كان حادثا مثله . الشنواني .
Reflection on the soul (nafs) and the world (al-‘ālam) such that it leads to knowledge of God, your essence (dhātuka) (1), because it is the nearest of things to you; due to His statement, the Most High: {And in yourselves, do you not see?} [al-Dhāriyāt 51:21]; {And certainly We created man from an extract of clay} [al-Mu’minūn 23:12]; so you infer by it. The author pointed out the obligation of contemplating the states of His essence; for reasons, first of which: it is the nearest of things to him; second: it has been narrated: “Whoever knows himself indeed knows his Lord,” i.e., whoever reflects on its marvels arrives at knowledge of its Maker; and it is said its meaning is: whoever knows himself by contingency (ḥudūth) and poverty knows his Lord by pre-eternity (qidam) and wealth. Indeed, if you look at the states of your essence which are near to you, you know that these states are changing, for you are sometimes healthy and sometimes sick, and you are sometimes attaining what you desire, and sometimes not attaining what you desire, and sometimes standing, and sometimes sitting, and sometimes sleeping, and so on of the states that befall you then disappear. And when you know that they are changing, you are certain that they are contingent (ḥāditha), and you are certain with that that your essence itself is contingent because it is inseparable from these contingent matters; it is impossible for an essence to be free from some of these matters. And when you know that your essence and its states are contingent, you are certain that there must be for it a Wise Maker, Necessary of Existence (wājib al-wujūd), Universal in Knowledge, Perfect in Power and Will. This is the way of a person’s inference, by reflection and contemplation on the states of his essence, to the necessity of the existence of a Wise Maker. Likewise, reflection on the states of the two worlds, the higher and the lower, for he will find each of them encompassed by specific directions and definite places; and he will find some of them moving and some stationary, some luminous and some dark; and he will find that these states change to their opposites. This is an indication of contingency, and it is an indication of the need for a Maker. And God, the Most High, censured those who abandon thought concerning their own affair by His statement, the Exalted: {And in yourselves, do you not see?} [al-Dhāriyāt 51:21] — He intends, the Exalted, and God knows best — “And in yourselves are signs indicating your contingency and your need for a Maker who brings into existence, qualified with all attributes of perfection. Do you leave off reflection upon them so that you do not see?” And this interrogative is in the sense of negation, i.e., it is not fitting for you to leave off reflection on the states of your selves. And the Exalted alerted to the obligation of reflection on the two worlds, the higher and the lower, by His statement: {Indeed in the creation of the heavens and the earth, and the alternation of the night and the day, and the ships which sail through the sea with that which benefits people, and what God has sent down from the sky of water, giving life thereby to the earth after its death, and dispersing therein every creature, and the directing of the winds and the clouds subjected between the sky and the earth, are signs for a people who reason} [al-Baqarah 2:164]. (1) His statement: (in the states of your essence) — he indicated by that that “reflect” (unẓur) is in the sense of “contemplate,” because it is transitive with “fī”; and it is possible that “ilā” in his statement “to yourself” remains in its original sense, such that reflection includes the meaning of directing or paying attention. And that what is meant by reflection on the essence is contemplation of its states, for it has no meaning other than this; or that what is meant by “the self” (nafs) is the essence (dhāt). (al-Shanwānī). Reflection on the soul and the world such that it leads to knowledge of God, concerning the necessity of the existence of your Maker and His attributes, for it (1) (i.e., your soul) includes hearing, sight, speech, length, width, depth, contentment, anger, whiteness, redness, blackness, knowledge, ignorance (2), faith, disbelief (3), pleasure, pain (4), and other things beyond count; all of them are changing, coming out from non-existence into existence, and from existence into non-existence; that is evidence of contingency and the need for a Wise Maker, Necessary of Existence, Universal in Knowledge, Perfect in Power and Will. Thus they are contingent, subsisting in the essence, inseparable from it; and it (the essence) and the concomitant of the contingent are also contingent (5). And he indicated another way by which reflection leads to knowledge of the necessity of the existence of the Maker and His attributes by his statement: (1) His statement: (for it) i.e., your soul, including — a cause for his statement “so you infer.” (al-Amīr). (2) His statement: (and ignorance) i.e., compound ignorance; as for simple ignorance, it is not an accident because it is non-existence, and the accident (ʿaraḍ) is particular to existence. (3) His statement: (and disbelief) — it is an accident if interpreted as denial; if interpreted as absence of faith, it is of the category of non-existence. His statement: (and pleasure and pain) — pleasure and pain are two perceptions: the first is perception of what is good for it, and pain is perception of what is evil for it; perception is an accident because it is among existing things changing from existence to non-existence and vice versa. (al-Amīr and al-Shanwānī). (4) His statement: (and the concomitant of the contingent is contingent) — what is meant by “the concomitant” (mulāzim) is the essence, and by “the contingent” (al-ḥādith) is the accidents; the meaning is: the essence inseparable from the contingent accidents is contingent like them. He used “concomitant” for “that which is concomitant” (malzūm). i.e., the malzūm of the contingent is contingent. For the concomitance between two things is that each of them is concomitant to the other. And your specific essence is not concomitant to the genera of those attributes, for genera of those attributes exist in other than your self. What he mentioned is in the strength of two premises, their estimation: your self is that which is concomitant to contingent attributes; and every malzūm of contingent attributes is contingent; it follows that your essence is contingent. The proof of the minor premise is what preceded; the proof of the major is that the malzūm of the contingent does not precede it, otherwise the malzūm would be found without the concomitant, and if it does not precede it, it is contingent like it. (al-Shanwānī).
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This passage is a commentary explaining the proof for God's existence and attributes through reflection (naẓar) on the self (nafs) and the world (ʿālam). It begins by stating that such reflection leads to knowledge of God, and specifically recommends starting with the self because it is the nearest object of study ('because it is the nearest of things to you'). The author then provides two reasons for this: first, the self is closest; second, a prophetic tradition states 'Whoever knows himself knows his Lord,' interpreted as reflecting on one's own marvelous composition leading to knowledge of the Maker, or recognizing one's contingency and need for a Pre-eternal and Self-Sufficient Creator. The argument proceeds by observing the changing states of one's own self (health and sickness, desire and lack, standing and sitting, etc.). Because these states constantly change, they are contingent (ḥāditha). Since the self is inseparable from such contingent states, the self itself must also be contingent. A contingent being requires a cause (muḥdith) who is Wise, Necessary of Existence (wājib al-wujūd), All-Knowing, All-Powerful, and Willing. This is the first way of inference. The passage then moves to a second way: reflecting on the two worlds (higher and lower). One observes that everything in them is limited in direction and place, some moving, some still, some luminous, some dark, and all change to their opposites. This change indicates contingency and thus the need for a Maker. The commentator supports this with Qur'anic verses: 'And in yourselves, do you not see?' (51:21) and a long verse from al-Baqarah (2:164) about signs in creation. The commentary continues with an explanation of the verse about the self, emphasizing that the interrogative is negative, i.e., you should not neglect reflection. The passage then returns to the self in a more detailed analysis on page 172, listing the attributes found in the self (hearing, sight, speech, dimensions, emotions, colors, knowledge, ignorance, faith, disbelief, pleasure, pain, etc.). All these attributes change—they come from non-existence into existence and vice versa. This change is evidence of their contingency and of the need for a Maker. Furthermore, these attributes inhere in the self, so the self is also contingent as the locus of contingent accidents. The commentator adds a logical formulation: the self is the malzūm (that which is concomitant) of contingent attributes; every malzūm of contingent things is itself contingent, so the self is contingent. A final note clarifies that the relation is not reciprocal; the self's specific essence is not a concomitant of the genera of those attributes since the same genera exist in other things. This passage thus presents a comprehensive argument from contingency (ḥudūth) based on observable change in both the self and the external world, establishing the necessity of a Creator with attributes of perfection.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that only some parts of the world change, or that the world as a whole is eternal.
Correction: The argument observes that all aspects of the world are subject to change and limitation, and the Quran commands reflection on these signs, proving that the entire world is contingent.

Checkpoint
What does the change of states in the world (from moving to still, luminous to dark) demonstrate according to the passage?
A) That the world is eternal.
B) That the world is contingent and needs a Maker.
C) That the world is self-sufficient.
D) That the world is purposeless.

Key Takeaway
The changing and limited nature of the world proves its contingency and therefore requires a Creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at the stars, the changing weather, your own heartbeat, do you genuinely sense that these things are contingent and in need of a Maker, or do you take them for granted as self-explanatory? What would it mean for your daily life if you truly saw each thing as a sign?
?
The verse says: 'And in yourselves, do you not see?' What specific signs of contingency do you see in yourself right now—your age, your health, your thoughts—that require a Creator? How does ignoring these signs affect your relationship with God?
?
You believe in God, but do you live as though the world is contingent and He is the only necessary being? Reflect on a time this week when your actions or worries suggested you forgot this. What would change if you remembered the contingency of everything?

Method of Learning
intermediate
6 min
The Self's Attributes and the Logical Proof of Contingency
Detailed analysis of self's attributes and logical proof of contingency

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the enumeration of the self's many changing attributes provides a detailed logical proof that the self is contingent and requires a Creator.

Core Idea
Every attribute we have changes, proving we are contingent; and since we cannot exist without some attributes, our very self is contingent.

This passage expands on the previous argument by listing the many attributes that inhere in the self: sensory faculties, dimensions, emotions, colors, knowledge, belief, pleasure, and pain. All of these change, coming from non-existence to existence and back. This change is evidence of their contingency. Since these attributes subsist in the self, the self itself is contingent. The passage also presents a formal syllogism: the self is the malzūm of contingent attributes, and every malzūm of contingent things is itself contingent, therefore the self is contingent.

Guided Path
1
Read the list of attributes
The passage enumerates many attributes: hearing, sight, speech, dimensions (length, width, depth), emotions (contentment, anger), colors (white, red, black), knowledge, ignorance, faith, disbelief, pleasure, pain, etc. Note the diversity.
2
Understand why these attributes prove contingency
Each attribute is observed to change—it comes into existence and disappears. That means it is not self-existent; it is contingent. The passage states this explicitly.
3
Grasp the logical relationship between self and attributes
These attributes subsist in the self (they are 'قائمة بالذات لازمة لها'). The self is inseparable from having some attributes. Therefore, if the attributes are contingent, the self must also be contingent.
4
Follow the syllogistic formulation
The commentary provides two premises: (1) Your self is malzūm (that which is concomitant) of contingent attributes. (2) Every malzūm of contingent attributes is itself contingent. Conclusion: Your self is contingent. Understand the proof of each premise.

النَّظرُ في النَّفْسِ والعَالَم بحيث يوصل إلى معرفة الله ذاتكَ (۱) ، لأنَّها أقرب الأشياء إليكَ ؛ لقوله تعالى: ﴿وَفِي أَنفُسِكُمْ أَفَلَا تُبْصِرُونَ﴾ [الذاريات: ۲۱] ﴿وَلَقَدْ خَلَقْنَا الإِنسَانَ مِن سُلَلَةٍ مِّن طِينٍ﴾ [المؤمنون: ۱۲] فَتَستَدل بها. أشار المصنف بذكر وجوب التفكر في أحوال ذاته ؛ لأمور أولها أنها أقرب الأشياء إليه ، وثانيها: أنه قد ورد: «مَن عرف نفسه فقد عرف ربه» أي من تفكر في بدائعها توصل إلى معرفة صانعها، وقيل معناه من عرف نفسه بالحدوث والفقر عرف ربه بالقدم والغنى، ألا أنك لو نظرت إلى أحوال ذاتك القريبة منك علمت أن أحوالها هذه متغيرة ، فأنت تارة معافى وتارة مريض، وأنت تارة واجد ما تريد، وتارة غير واجد لما تريد، وتارة قائم، وتارة قاعد، وتارة نائم، إلى غير ذلك من الأحوال التي تعرض لك ثم تزول، وإذا علمت أنها متغيرة أيقنت أنها حادثة ، وأيقنت مع ذلك أن ذاتك نفسها حادثة لأنها ملازمة لهذه الأمور الحادثة لا يمكن أن تنفك ذات عن بعض هذه الأمور ، وإذا علمت أن ذاتك وأحوالها حادثة أيقنت أن لا بد لها من محدث حكيم واجب الوجود عام العلم تام القدرة والإرادة ؛ فهذا طريق استدلال الإنسان بالنظر والتفكر في أحوال ذاته على وجوب وجود صانع حكيم، وكذلك التفكر في أحوال العالَميْنِ العلوي والسفلي فإنه سيجد كلا منهما مشمولا بجهات مخصوصة وأمكنة معينة، وسيجد بعضها متحركا وبعضها ساكنا، وبعضه نورانيا وبعضه ظلمانيا ، وسيجد أن هذه الأحوال تتغير إلى مقابلاتها، وهذا أمارة الحدوث، وهو أمارة الاحتياج إلى مُحدث، وقد نعى الله تعالى على من ترك الفكر في أمر نفسه بقوله سبحانه: ﴿وَفِي أَنفُسِكُمْ أَفَلَا تُبْصِرُونَ﴾ [الذاريات: ۲۱] يريد سبحانه ـ والله أعلم ـ وفي أنفسكم آيات دالة على حدوثكم وافتقاركم إلى محدث يوجد، متصف بجميع صفات الكمال ، أتتركون التفكر فيها فلا تبصرون. وهذا الاستفهام بمعنى النفي، أي: لا ينبغي لكم ترك النظر في أحوال ذواتكم، ونبّه سبحانه على وجوب التفكر في العالمين العلوي والسفلي بقوله: ﴿إِنَّ فِي خَلْقِ السَّمَوَاتِ وَالْأَرْضِ وَاخْتِلَفِ الَّيْلِ وَالنَّهَارِ وَالْفُلْكِ الَّتِي تَجْرِي فِي الْبَحْرِ بِمَا يَنفَعُ النَّاسَ وَمَا أَنزَلَ اللَّهُ مِنَ السَّمَاءِ مِن مَّاءٍ فَأَحْيَا بِهِ الْأَرْضَ بَعْدَ مَوْتِهَا وَبَثَّ فِيهَا مِن كُلِّ دَابَّةٍ وَتَصْرِيفِ الرِّيحِ وَالسَّحَابِ الْمُسَخَّرِ بَيْنَ السَّمَاءِ وَالْأَرْضِ لَآيَاتٍ لِقَوْمٍ يَعْقِلُونَ﴾ [البقرة: ١٦٤]. (1) قوله: (في أحوال ذاتك) أشار بذلك إلى أنَّ (انظر) بمعنى: تفكر، لأنه الذي يتعدى بمعنى (في) ويحتمل أن تكون (إلى) في قوله : (إلى نفسك) باقية على أصلها، على أنَّ النظر متضمن معنى توجه أو انتبه. وأن المراد بالنظر في الذات التفكر في أحوالها ؛ إذ ليس له معنى إلا هذا ، أو أن المراد بالنفس الذات . الشنواني. النَّظرُ في النَّفس والعالم بحيث يوصل إلى معرفة الله على وجوب وجود صانعك وصفاته، فإنها(۱) مشتملة على سمع، وبصر، وكلام، وطولٍ وعَرض، وعُمق، ورضا، وغضب، وبياض، وحمرة، وسَوادٍ ، وعِلمٍ ، وجهل (۲) ، وإيمان ، وكُفر ، ولذَّةٍ، وألم (۳) ، وغير ذلك مما لا يُحصى، وكلُّها متغيّرة وخارجةٌ مِن العدم إلى الوجود، ومن الوجود إلى العدم، وذلك دليل الحدوثِ والافتقار إلى صانع حكيم واجب الوجود ، عام العلم ، تامّ القدرة والإرادة، فتكون حادثةً، قائمة بالذات لازمة لها، وهي وملازم الحادثِ حادث أيضا (٤). وأشار إلى طريق آخرَ يُوصل النَّظرُ فيه إلى معرفة وجوب وجود الصانع وصفاته بقوله: (۱) قوله: (فإنها ) أي : نفسكَ ، مشتملة تعليل لقوله: تستدل . الأمير . (۲) قوله: (وجهل أي مركب ، وأما البسيط فليس بعرض؛ لأنه عدم، والعرض مخصوص بالوجود. (۳) قوله : (وكُفر) هو عرض إن فُسر بالجحد، وإن فُسر بعدم الإيمان فهو من قبيل العدم. قوله: (ولذة وألم اللذة والألم إدراكان ، فالأول : إدراك ما هو خير له ، والألم: هو إدراك ما هو شر له ، والإدراك عرض لأنه من الأمور الموجودة بتغيير من وجود إلى عدم وبالعكس. الأمير والشنواني. (٤) قوله : ( وملازم الحادث حادث المراد بالمُلازم الذات، وبالحادث الأعراض، والمعنى: الذات الملازمة للأعراض الحادثة حادثة مثلها ، فقد أطلق الملازم على الملزوم ، أي : وملزوم الحادث حادث ؛ إذ الملازمة بين الشيئين أن يكون كل منهما لازما للآخر، وليست ذاتك المخصوصة لازمة لأجناس تلك الصفات ؛ إذ أجناس تلك الصفات توجد في غير نفسك، وما ذكره في قوة مقدمتين تقديرهما هكذا: نفسك ملزومة لصفات حادثة ، وكل ملزوم للصفات الحادثة فهو حادث ، ينتج نفسك حادثة، ودليل الصغرى ما تقدم، ودليل الكبرى: أن ملزوم الحادث لا يسبقه، وإلا لوجد الملزوم بدون اللازم، وإن لم يسبقه كان حادثا مثله . الشنواني .
Reflection on the soul (nafs) and the world (al-‘ālam) such that it leads to knowledge of God, your essence (dhātuka) (1), because it is the nearest of things to you; due to His statement, the Most High: {And in yourselves, do you not see?} [al-Dhāriyāt 51:21]; {And certainly We created man from an extract of clay} [al-Mu’minūn 23:12]; so you infer by it. The author pointed out the obligation of contemplating the states of His essence; for reasons, first of which: it is the nearest of things to him; second: it has been narrated: “Whoever knows himself indeed knows his Lord,” i.e., whoever reflects on its marvels arrives at knowledge of its Maker; and it is said its meaning is: whoever knows himself by contingency (ḥudūth) and poverty knows his Lord by pre-eternity (qidam) and wealth. Indeed, if you look at the states of your essence which are near to you, you know that these states are changing, for you are sometimes healthy and sometimes sick, and you are sometimes attaining what you desire, and sometimes not attaining what you desire, and sometimes standing, and sometimes sitting, and sometimes sleeping, and so on of the states that befall you then disappear. And when you know that they are changing, you are certain that they are contingent (ḥāditha), and you are certain with that that your essence itself is contingent because it is inseparable from these contingent matters; it is impossible for an essence to be free from some of these matters. And when you know that your essence and its states are contingent, you are certain that there must be for it a Wise Maker, Necessary of Existence (wājib al-wujūd), Universal in Knowledge, Perfect in Power and Will. This is the way of a person’s inference, by reflection and contemplation on the states of his essence, to the necessity of the existence of a Wise Maker. Likewise, reflection on the states of the two worlds, the higher and the lower, for he will find each of them encompassed by specific directions and definite places; and he will find some of them moving and some stationary, some luminous and some dark; and he will find that these states change to their opposites. This is an indication of contingency, and it is an indication of the need for a Maker. And God, the Most High, censured those who abandon thought concerning their own affair by His statement, the Exalted: {And in yourselves, do you not see?} [al-Dhāriyāt 51:21] — He intends, the Exalted, and God knows best — “And in yourselves are signs indicating your contingency and your need for a Maker who brings into existence, qualified with all attributes of perfection. Do you leave off reflection upon them so that you do not see?” And this interrogative is in the sense of negation, i.e., it is not fitting for you to leave off reflection on the states of your selves. And the Exalted alerted to the obligation of reflection on the two worlds, the higher and the lower, by His statement: {Indeed in the creation of the heavens and the earth, and the alternation of the night and the day, and the ships which sail through the sea with that which benefits people, and what God has sent down from the sky of water, giving life thereby to the earth after its death, and dispersing therein every creature, and the directing of the winds and the clouds subjected between the sky and the earth, are signs for a people who reason} [al-Baqarah 2:164]. (1) His statement: (in the states of your essence) — he indicated by that that “reflect” (unẓur) is in the sense of “contemplate,” because it is transitive with “fī”; and it is possible that “ilā” in his statement “to yourself” remains in its original sense, such that reflection includes the meaning of directing or paying attention. And that what is meant by reflection on the essence is contemplation of its states, for it has no meaning other than this; or that what is meant by “the self” (nafs) is the essence (dhāt). (al-Shanwānī). Reflection on the soul and the world such that it leads to knowledge of God, concerning the necessity of the existence of your Maker and His attributes, for it (1) (i.e., your soul) includes hearing, sight, speech, length, width, depth, contentment, anger, whiteness, redness, blackness, knowledge, ignorance (2), faith, disbelief (3), pleasure, pain (4), and other things beyond count; all of them are changing, coming out from non-existence into existence, and from existence into non-existence; that is evidence of contingency and the need for a Wise Maker, Necessary of Existence, Universal in Knowledge, Perfect in Power and Will. Thus they are contingent, subsisting in the essence, inseparable from it; and it (the essence) and the concomitant of the contingent are also contingent (5). And he indicated another way by which reflection leads to knowledge of the necessity of the existence of the Maker and His attributes by his statement: (1) His statement: (for it) i.e., your soul, including — a cause for his statement “so you infer.” (al-Amīr). (2) His statement: (and ignorance) i.e., compound ignorance; as for simple ignorance, it is not an accident because it is non-existence, and the accident (ʿaraḍ) is particular to existence. (3) His statement: (and disbelief) — it is an accident if interpreted as denial; if interpreted as absence of faith, it is of the category of non-existence. His statement: (and pleasure and pain) — pleasure and pain are two perceptions: the first is perception of what is good for it, and pain is perception of what is evil for it; perception is an accident because it is among existing things changing from existence to non-existence and vice versa. (al-Amīr and al-Shanwānī). (4) His statement: (and the concomitant of the contingent is contingent) — what is meant by “the concomitant” (mulāzim) is the essence, and by “the contingent” (al-ḥādith) is the accidents; the meaning is: the essence inseparable from the contingent accidents is contingent like them. He used “concomitant” for “that which is concomitant” (malzūm). i.e., the malzūm of the contingent is contingent. For the concomitance between two things is that each of them is concomitant to the other. And your specific essence is not concomitant to the genera of those attributes, for genera of those attributes exist in other than your self. What he mentioned is in the strength of two premises, their estimation: your self is that which is concomitant to contingent attributes; and every malzūm of contingent attributes is contingent; it follows that your essence is contingent. The proof of the minor premise is what preceded; the proof of the major is that the malzūm of the contingent does not precede it, otherwise the malzūm would be found without the concomitant, and if it does not precede it, it is contingent like it. (al-Shanwānī).
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This passage is a commentary explaining the proof for God's existence and attributes through reflection (naẓar) on the self (nafs) and the world (ʿālam). It begins by stating that such reflection leads to knowledge of God, and specifically recommends starting with the self because it is the nearest object of study ('because it is the nearest of things to you'). The author then provides two reasons for this: first, the self is closest; second, a prophetic tradition states 'Whoever knows himself knows his Lord,' interpreted as reflecting on one's own marvelous composition leading to knowledge of the Maker, or recognizing one's contingency and need for a Pre-eternal and Self-Sufficient Creator. The argument proceeds by observing the changing states of one's own self (health and sickness, desire and lack, standing and sitting, etc.). Because these states constantly change, they are contingent (ḥāditha). Since the self is inseparable from such contingent states, the self itself must also be contingent. A contingent being requires a cause (muḥdith) who is Wise, Necessary of Existence (wājib al-wujūd), All-Knowing, All-Powerful, and Willing. This is the first way of inference. The passage then moves to a second way: reflecting on the two worlds (higher and lower). One observes that everything in them is limited in direction and place, some moving, some still, some luminous, some dark, and all change to their opposites. This change indicates contingency and thus the need for a Maker. The commentator supports this with Qur'anic verses: 'And in yourselves, do you not see?' (51:21) and a long verse from al-Baqarah (2:164) about signs in creation. The commentary continues with an explanation of the verse about the self, emphasizing that the interrogative is negative, i.e., you should not neglect reflection. The passage then returns to the self in a more detailed analysis on page 172, listing the attributes found in the self (hearing, sight, speech, dimensions, emotions, colors, knowledge, ignorance, faith, disbelief, pleasure, pain, etc.). All these attributes change—they come from non-existence into existence and vice versa. This change is evidence of their contingency and of the need for a Maker. Furthermore, these attributes inhere in the self, so the self is also contingent as the locus of contingent accidents. The commentator adds a logical formulation: the self is the malzūm (that which is concomitant) of contingent attributes; every malzūm of contingent things is itself contingent, so the self is contingent. A final note clarifies that the relation is not reciprocal; the self's specific essence is not a concomitant of the genera of those attributes since the same genera exist in other things. This passage thus presents a comprehensive argument from contingency (ḥudūth) based on observable change in both the self and the external world, establishing the necessity of a Creator with attributes of perfection.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the self's contingency is proven by a single attribute changing, but the argument requires that the self is always accompanying some contingent attribute.
Correction: The argument is that the self cannot be free from attributes that are constantly changing. This constant association with the contingent makes the self itself contingent.

Checkpoint
In the syllogism presented, what is the major premise?
A) The self possesses many changing attributes.
B) Every malzūm of contingent attributes is itself contingent.
C) The self is contingent because it is created.
D) Attributes change from non-existence to existence.

Key Takeaway
By listing the many changing attributes of the self and their logical relationship, the passage provides a rigorous proof that the self is contingent and therefore requires a Creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just read that every one of your attributes—your sight, your knowledge, your emotions—is fleeting, coming from nothing and returning to nothing. Where do you place your sense of stability? In what do you trust to remain constant?
?
If even your own self is contingent and dependent on a Maker, what would change in your daily life if you truly believed you are not your own source? How would you start your morning differently?
?
The proof shows that your very existence is a chain of moments held by another. Are you living as if that is true, or are you acting as if you own your own life? What would it mean to surrender that ownership right now?

Reflection on Self and the Two Worlds
Defines nazar, divides world into higher and lower, explains order flexibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the method of theological reflection (nazar) as defined by the author, including its objects (self, higher world, lower world) and the non-essential nature of the order presented.

Core Idea
Reflection on self and the universe is the path to knowledge of God, and any order works.

This lesson introduces the foundational method of nazar (reflection) in Islamic theology. The author explains that by reflecting on oneself and the two realms of the world—the higher celestial realm and the lower terrestrial realm—one arrives at knowledge of God. Interestingly, the order in which you reflect is not critical; what matters is the recognition of contingency in all things, pointing to a Necessary Creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the use of 'thumma' (then) – pay attention to how it is later qualified.
2
Identify the three objects of reflection
The passage lists three things to reflect on: the self, the higher world, and the lower world. Find how each is defined and what signs of God are pointed out in them.
The higher world includes celestial bodies; the lower world includes earth and atmosphere.
3
Understand why the order is not essential
The author explicitly states that reversing or rearranging the order does not invalidate the reflection. The word 'thumma' indicates only a rhetorical order, not a logical necessity. Consider why the higher world is mentioned first despite being farther from experience.
The Qur'anic verse (2:164) is cited to show precedence is by way of following divine guidance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النَّظرُ في النَّفْسِ والعَالَم بحيث يوصل إلى معرفة الله (ثُمَّ انْتَقِلِ) بعد نظركَ في نفسكَ (لِلْعَالَمِ) أي: للنظر في أحوال العالم (العُلْوِيَّ) وهو ما سوى الله تعالى وصفاته من الموجودات، سُمِّي به: لأنَّه علمٌ على وجود الصانع تعالى؛ فيُعلم به، ويُستَدَلَّ به عليه؛ لأنَّ في كلِّ علامةً تدلّ على قدرة الصانع وإرادته وعلمه وحياته وحكمته، والمراد بالعلوي: ما ارتفع من الفلكيَّات من سماواتٍ وكواكب وغيرها؛ لأنَّك تجده مشمولا لجهاتٍ مخصوصةٍ وأمكنة مُعيَّنة، وبعضه متحرِّكاً، وبعضه ساكنا، وبعضه نورانيا، وبعضه ظُلمانيا، وذلك دليل الحدوث والافتقار إلى صانع مُنزَّه عن مماثلة لمصنوعه ذاتا وصفات. (ثُمَّ انتقل بالنظر في أحوال العالم (السفلي) وهو كلُّ ما نَزل عن الفلكيات إلى مُنقطع العالم، كالهواء والسحاب والأرض وما فيها. ولا تتوقف صحَّةُ النَّظر على الترتيب الذي ذكره المصنف رحمه الله تعالى، بل لو عكس فأخَّرَ المُقدَّم وقدَّم المؤخَّر أو وَسَطه لصح أيضًا، فلتكن «ثمَّ» للترتيب الذكري. وتقديمُ العالم العلوي على السُّفلي وإن كان أقرب إلى الاعتبار؛ اقتداءً حيث قدمه عليه في مقام الاعتبار، قال تعالى: {إِنَّ فِي خَلْقِ السَّمَوَاتِ وَالْأَرْضِ} [البقرة: 164] الآية.
Reflection on the self and the world such that it leads to knowledge of God. Then move on, after your reflection on your self, to the world, meaning reflection on the states of the higher world, which is everything other than God Most High and His attributes among existent things. It is so named because it is a sign of the existence of the Maker Most High; He is known through it and inferred from it, because in every part there is a sign indicating the power of the Maker, His will, His knowledge, His life, and His wisdom. By 'higher' is meant what is elevated from the celestial things—skies, planets, and others—because you find it encompassed by specific directions and particular places, some of it moving, some stationary, some luminous, some dark, and that is evidence of origination and need for a Maker exalted above likeness to His creation in essence and attributes. Then move on by reflecting on the states of the lower world, which is everything that descends from the celestial things to the end of the world, like air, clouds, earth, and what is in them. The validity of reflection does not depend on the order mentioned by the author—may God have mercy on him—but rather if he reversed, delaying the first and advancing the last, or intermediated, it would also be valid. So let 'thumma' be for rhetorical order. The prioritization of the higher over the lower world, even if the lower is closer to consideration, is by way of following where He gave it priority in the context of consideration. He said: {Indeed, in the creation of the heavens and the earth} [Quran 2:164]—the verse.
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This passage defines and classifies the method of theological reflection (nazar). It begins by stating that reflection upon the self and the world leads to knowledge of God. Then it instructs to move from the self to the 'higher world,' which is defined as everything other than God and His attributes. This world is called 'higher' not only because of its physical elevation but because it serves as a sign pointing to the existence of the Maker—every part displays indications of His power, will, knowledge, life, and wisdom. The author details aspects of the higher world—its divisions of direction, motion, and light—all of which demonstrate contingency (ḥudūth) and need for a Creator who is absolutely unlike His creation. Next, it prescribes reflection on the 'lower world,' which includes everything beneath the celestial spheres down to the earth and its contents. However, the passage clarifies that the specified order (self, then higher, then lower) is not essential for the validity of reflection; one could reverse or rearrange the sequence and the inference would still be sound. The word 'thumma' (then) here indicates merely a rhetorical or presentational order, not a necessary logical sequence. The reason for mentioning the higher world before the lower, despite the lower being closer to our immediate experience, is to emulate the Qur'anic order, as in the verse from Surah al-Baqarah. Thus, the passage emphasizes the flexibility of the method while maintaining the theological importance of both worlds as signs of God.

Key Terms
النظر
العالم العلوي
العالم السفلي
الحدوث
الترتيب الذكري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the order of reflection (self, then higher world, then lower world) is a logical necessity for the argument to be valid.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that the validity of reflection does not depend on this order; reversing or rearranging still yields a valid inference. The order is merely rhetorical and follows Qur'anic usage.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the primary purpose of reflecting on the self and the world?
A) To gain scientific knowledge of the universe
B) To lead to knowledge of God
C) To prove the existence of the soul
D) To establish the order of creation

Key Takeaway
The method of nazar is flexible in order but fixed in its goal: recognizing signs of the Creator in oneself and both realms of the world.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at your own body—its complexity, its limits, its constant need for maintenance—do you truly pause to see the hand of the Creator, or does it all feel too familiar to move you? What would it take for you to make that reflection a daily habit that changes how you live?
?
You have likely recited or heard Qur'an 2:164 many times, but have you ever stopped to examine a single part of creation—a leaf, a star, your own heartbeat—as evidence of God's attributes? If not, what is stopping you from doing that right now?
?
The author says the order of reflection is flexible, but your heart knows it is easy to neglect this duty entirely. If you were to commit to just five minutes of nazar today, what specific sign from the higher or lower world would you contemplate, and what do you expect it to reveal about your relationship with your Creator?

The Flexibility of Contemplation Order in Knowing God
Commentary on the flexibility of contemplation order.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the validity of contemplating creation to know God does not depend on a fixed order, and that the Quranic model of mentioning heavens before earth is a preferred emulation.

Core Idea
The order of contemplation is flexible; the Quranic sequence is a guide, not a requirement.

This lesson examines a key methodological point in Islamic theology: is there a required sequence for contemplating the self, the heavens, and the earth to arrive at knowledge of God? The commentator clarifies that while a preferred order exists (following the Quran), the validity of the reasoning does not hinge on following that order precisely.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the use of 'thumma' and its implications.
2
Identify the main claim about order
The author states that the soundness of contemplation does not depend on the specific order mentioned. Find this statement and understand its significance.
This is the core flexibility point.
3
Understand 'thumma' as order of mention
The passage explains that 'thumma' here indicates the order of mention (al-tartīb al-dhikrī), not a necessary causal or temporal sequence. Reflect on why this distinction matters.
4
Reflect on the Quranic evidence
The author cites Quran 2:164, which mentions heavens before earth. Consider why the author uses this verse to support the preferred order, and how emulation is encouraged.
The verse shows that the order is not arbitrary but follows divine guidance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النَّظرُ في النَّفْسِ والعَالَم بحيث يوصل إلى معرفة الله الفلكيات إلى مُنقطع العالم ، كالهواء والسحاب والأرض وما فيها. ولا تتوقف صحَّةُ النَّظر على الترتيب الذي ذكره المصنف رحمه الله تعالى، بل لو عكس فأخَّرَ المُقدَّم وقدَّم المؤخَّر، أو وَشَطه لصح أيضًا، فلتكن «ثمَّ» للترتيب الذكري. وتقديمُ العالم العلوي على السُّفليّ وإِنْ كانَ أقرب إلى الاعتبار ؛ اقتداء حيث قدمه عليه في مقام الاعتبار ، قال تعالى: ﴿إِنَّ فِي خَلْقِ السَّمَوَاتِ وَالْأَرْضِ ﴾ [البقرة: ١٦٤] الآية .
Contemplation of the self and the world in a way that leads to knowledge of God, the celestial bodies to the boundary of the world, like air, clouds, earth, and what is in them. The soundness of contemplation does not depend on the order mentioned by the author (may Allah have mercy on him). Rather, if one reversed it, delaying the advanced and advancing the delayed, or omitted something, it would still be sound. Thus, 'thumma' here is for the order of mention. The advancement of the upper world over the lower, even though the latter is closer to consideration, is in emulation of where He advanced it in the stance of consideration. He, the Exalted, said: 'Indeed, in the creation of the heavens and the earth...' [Quran 2:164] the verse.
Source pages p. 174

Commentary
This passage addresses the proper sequence of contemplation (النظر) to arrive at knowledge of God. The author states that one should contemplate the self and the world, including celestial spheres, air, clouds, earth, etc. He asserts that the validity of this contemplation does not depend on a fixed order; one could reverse the order or skip some elements and it would still be valid. The word 'thumma' (then) in the original text is thus interpreted as indicating the order of mention (الترتيب الذكري), not a necessary causal or temporal order. The author prefers to place the upper world (heavens) before the lower world (earth), even though the lower world is closer to our experience, because this follows the Quranic order, as in the verse from Surah Al-Baqarah (2:164) which mentions heavens before earth.

Key Terms

نفس
عالم علوي
عالم سفلي
ترتيب ذكري

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that the order of contemplation (self, then heavens, then earth) is rigidly required for valid reasoning to prove God's existence.
Correction: The passage clarifies that the order is flexible; one can reverse or omit parts and the contemplation remains valid. The Quranic sequence is a preferred model, not a condition.

Checkpoint
What does the author say about the order of contemplation (النظر)?
A) It must follow a strict sequence.

C) It must always start with the lower world.

Key Takeaway
The validity of reasoning from creation to God does not depend on a fixed order; the Quranic sequence is a model to emulate, not a condition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Will you now release that fear and seek God through whatever part of His creation speaks to your heart today?
?
The Quranic order is presented as a model to emulate, not a requirement. Are you following the Quran out of habit or out of conscious emulation of Allah's guidance? How can you make your contemplation more intentional and less mechanical?

The Three Divisions of Tawhid
Sanusi enumerates the three categories of theological topics.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the topics of tawhid are classified into Ilahiyyat, Nubuwwat, and Sam'iyyat.

Core Idea
Theology is divided into three: God, Prophets, and Revelation-dependent matters.

Before diving into detailed arguments, Sanusi first gives us the map of the discipline. He divides the entire subject of tawhid into three clear categories, each with its own subject matter. This classification helps us know what belongs where and prevents confusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic text once. Notice the three terms: إلهيات, نبوات, سمعيات.
These are the three pillars.
2
Identify the scope of each
Ilahiyyat deals with God, Nubuwwat with prophethood, Sam'iyyat with what comes only from revelation.
Sam'iyyat includes afterlife details.
3
Reflect on the warning
The passage ends with a caution about research in Sam'iyyat leading to shirk if not careful.
This shows the practical concern.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد انقسمت مباحث هذا الفن ثلاثة أقسام: إلهيات: وهي المسائل التي يبحث فيها عن الإله. ونبوات: وهي المسائل المبحوث فيها عن النبوة وأحوالها. وسمعيات: وهي المسائل التي لا تتلقى أحكامها إلا من السمع، ولا بحث فيها إشراك أي مؤد للكفر. قوله: (عن الإله) أي من حيث صفاته، وإلا فالمحققون قد أجمعوا على عدم وقوع معرفة الكنه، واختلفوا في الجواز؛ والأليق الاستحالة، كما في شرح الكبرى عن الإمام الغزالي، فإن الحادث يقصر بالطبع عن عظيم هذا المقام، سبحان من لا يعلم قدره غيره، ولا يلزم من الرؤية علم الكنه؛ فإنها بلا كيف، والعجز عن ذات الله إدراك، أي: علم بما هو المطلوب شرعا من الوقف وعمل به.
And the topics of this art have been divided into three sections: Ilahiyyat (divine matters): which are the issues investigated concerning the divine. Nubuwwat (prophethood matters): which are the issues investigated concerning prophethood and its states. Sam'iyyat (hearsay matters): which are the issues whose rulings are not received except from hearing (revelation), and there is no research in it that associates partners [with Allah] i.e., leads to disbelief. His saying: 'concerning the divine' means in terms of His attributes, for otherwise the verifiers have unanimously agreed upon the impossibility of truly actual essential knowledge (of His essence), and they differed concerning the possibility; but the more fitting view is impossibility, as in the commentary on 'al-Kubra' from Imam al-Ghazali. For the contingent is by nature incapable of the greatness of this station. Exalted is He whom none knows His measure but Him. And vision does not necessitate essential knowledge; for it is without 'how', and the inability to comprehend Allah's essence is (itself) comprehension, i.e., knowledge of what is required legally: to stop and to act upon it.
Source pages p. 208

Commentary
This passage classifies the topics of the science of tawhid (theology) into three categories. First, Ilahiyyat (divine matters) which investigate God, specifically His attributes. The commentary notes that God's essence is unknowable; even the possibility of essential knowledge is disputed, but the stronger view is its impossibility. Vision of God (in the afterlife) does not entail essential knowledge because it is without modality. Second, Nubuwwat (prophethood matters) which investigate prophethood and its circumstances. Third, Sam'iyyat (hearsay matters) which rely solely on revelation, such as eschatological details. The passage warns that engaging in speculative research in these matters may lead to shirk. The footnote clarifies that when we say 'investigate the divine', it is only regarding attributes, not the essence, as the mind cannot grasp the essence.

Key Terms
إلهيات
نبوات
سمعيات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, into how many sections are the topics of this art divided?
A) Two
B) Three
C) Four
D) Five

Key Takeaway
The science of tawhid is organized into three main branches: Ilahiyyat, Nubuwwat, and Sam'iyyat, each with its own scope.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Sam'iyyat are matters you must accept without full rational proof. Is there an area of your faith where you struggle to submit to revelation without demanding intellectual certainty? What is that area, and how can you trust Allah's word over your own reasoning starting today?
?
The author warns that researching Sam'iyyat can lead to shirk. Have you ever tried to reason about the unseen (e.g., the nature of the afterlife or angels) beyond what has been revealed? How might that subtle pride in your intellect distance you from reliance on Allah? What will you do to guard your heart from this?
?
Consider the balance of the three divisions: Ilahiyyat, Nubuwwat, and Sam'iyyat. Do you give equal weight to learning about Allah, His prophets, and the unseen, or do you focus on one to the neglect of others? Which division do you need to strengthen in your daily study, and what concrete step will you take this week to address that?

On the Impossibility of Knowing Allah's Essence
Sanusi clarifies that Ilahiyyat is about attributes, not essence, and explains the impossibility of knowing the essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why it is impossible for created beings to achieve essential knowledge (ma'rifat al-kunnh) of Allah, and how this relates to the vision of Allah in the afterlife.

Core Idea
The created intellect cannot grasp the essence of the Creator.

In the previous lesson, we learned that Ilahiyyat investigates Allah's attributes, not His essence. Now the author explains why: the created intellect is fundamentally incapable of grasping the true reality of Allah. This is a key principle in Ash'ari theology, preserving divine transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the footnote passage
Read the Arabic text starting from 'قوله: (عن الإله)'. Understand the discussion on essence vs attributes.
2
Grasp the consensus
The verifiers agree that essential knowledge is impossible, though there is a dispute on its possibility.
The stronger view is impossibility.
3
Understand the nature of vision
Vision of Allah (ru'ya) does not entail essential knowledge because it is without modality (bila kayf).
This is a crucial distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد انقسمت مباحث هذا الفن ثلاثة أقسام: إلهيات: وهي المسائل التي يبحث فيها عن الإله. ونبوات: وهي المسائل المبحوث فيها عن النبوة وأحوالها. وسمعيات: وهي المسائل التي لا تتلقى أحكامها إلا من السمع، ولا بحث فيها إشراك أي مؤد للكفر. قوله: (عن الإله) أي من حيث صفاته، وإلا فالمحققون قد أجمعوا على عدم وقوع معرفة الكنه، واختلفوا في الجواز؛ والأليق الاستحالة، كما في شرح الكبرى عن الإمام الغزالي، فإن الحادث يقصر بالطبع عن عظيم هذا المقام، سبحان من لا يعلم قدره غيره، ولا يلزم من الرؤية علم الكنه؛ فإنها بلا كيف، والعجز عن ذات الله إدراك، أي: علم بما هو المطلوب شرعا من الوقف وعمل به.
And the topics of this art have been divided into three sections: Ilahiyyat (divine matters): which are the issues investigated concerning the divine. Nubuwwat (prophethood matters): which are the issues investigated concerning prophethood and its states. Sam'iyyat (hearsay matters): which are the issues whose rulings are not received except from hearing (revelation), and there is no research in it that associates partners [with Allah] i.e., leads to disbelief. His saying: 'concerning the divine' means in terms of His attributes, for otherwise the verifiers have unanimously agreed upon the impossibility of truly actual essential knowledge (of His essence), and they differed concerning the possibility; but the more fitting view is impossibility, as in the commentary on 'al-Kubra' from Imam al-Ghazali. For the contingent is by nature incapable of the greatness of this station. Exalted is He whom none knows His measure but Him. And vision does not necessitate essential knowledge; for it is without 'how', and the inability to comprehend Allah's essence is (itself) comprehension, i.e., knowledge of what is required legally: to stop and to act upon it.
Source pages p. 208

Commentary
This passage classifies the topics of the science of tawhid (theology) into three categories. First, Ilahiyyat (divine matters) which investigate God, specifically His attributes. The commentary notes that God's essence is unknowable; even the possibility of essential knowledge is disputed, but the stronger view is its impossibility. Vision of God (in the afterlife) does not entail essential knowledge because it is without modality. Second, Nubuwwat (prophethood matters) which investigate prophethood and its circumstances. Third, Sam'iyyat (hearsay matters) which rely solely on revelation, such as eschatological details. The passage warns that engaging in speculative research in these matters may lead to shirk. The footnote clarifies that when we say 'investigate the divine', it is only regarding attributes, not the essence, as the mind cannot grasp the essence.

الكنه
الصفات
الرؤية

Misconception: Some think that seeing Allah in paradise means knowing His essence.
Correction: Vision does not entail essential knowledge because it is without modality.

Checkpoint

B) Because the created is by nature incapable
C) Because the texts do not describe it
D) Because it leads to shirk

Key Takeaway
Essential knowledge of Allah is impossible for created beings due to their inherent limitation; even vision in the afterlife does not entail essential knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?

Where in your life do you need to stop trying to intellectually conquer Allah and instead act on what He has commanded? What specific step will you take today to act on what you know, rather than seeking more than what is possible?

Three Divisions of Tawhid Topics
Classification of tawhid topics into three sections

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three divisions of tawhid topics (Ilahiyyat, Nubuwwat, Sam'iyyat) and why existence is the foundational topic within Ilahiyyat.

Core Idea
Tawhid topics are divided into three: divine, prophetic, and scriptural matters.

The study of tawhid is systematically organized into three categories: matters concerning Allah, matters concerning prophets, and matters known only through revelation. This passage from the commentary establishes this classification and then explains why the author of the poem starts with existence. Understanding this structure helps you navigate the entire discipline of kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the author's classification come through before interpreting.
Notice the three terms: إلهيات, نبوات, سمعيات.
2
Identify the three divisions
The passage presents three divisions: Ilahiyyat (divine matters), Nubuwwat (prophethood matters), and Sam'iyyat (scriptural matters). Find each definition.
3
Understand why existence is foundational
The commentator explains that existence (wujud) is the foundation for rulings about necessity, impossibility, and possibility regarding Allah. Reflect on why this is the starting point.
This connects the classification to the beginning of the poem.

Why Existence Is the Starting Point in Divine Attributes
Explains why existence is the starting point and the metaphorical nature of 'origin'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author of Jawharat al-Tawhid began the discussion of divine attributes with existence, and why existence is considered the 'origin' for the three rational judgments about Allah.

Core Idea
Existence is the root of all rational judgments about Allah.

This passage explains a key methodological choice in kalam: why the topic of divine attributes starts with existence. The author argues that affirming Allah's existence is the foundation upon which all other judgments—what is necessary, impossible, and possible for Him—are built. The commentary also clarifies that calling existence an 'origin' is a conceptual metaphor, not a statement about real composition in Allah's attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the two footnotes marked (1) and (2)—they are commentary expansions.
2
Identify the reason for starting with existence
The passage states that existence is the 'origin' (أصل). Find the sentence that explains why the other judgments are branches (فرع) of existence.
The three branches are obligation (وجوب), impossibility (استحالة), and permissibility (جواز).
3
Understand the order of priorities
The first footnote (from Muhyī) elaborates on why existence is prioritized within divine inquiries. Notice the chain: divine matters > what is obligatory > existence.
It emphasizes that the judgment of obligation cannot be conceived without first affirming existence.
4
Reflect on the metaphor of 'origin'
The second footnote (from al-Amīr) warns that 'origin' when applied to Allah's attributes is metaphorical. Think about why using real priority language might lead to misunderstanding.
Allah's attributes have no similar or partner—they are unique.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
انقسام مباحث فنِّ التَّوحِيد إلى ثلاثة أقسام
فلذا شرع في تفصيل ما أجمله بقوله أولاً :
فَكُلُّ مَنْ كُلِّفَ شَرْعًا وَجَبَا عَلَيْهِ أَنْ يَعْرِفَ
.. البيت
وبدأ من القسم الأوّل (۱) بما هو الأصل (۲) ، وهو الوجود ؛ لأنَّ الحكم بوجوب الواجبات له تعالى، واستحالة ما يتنزّه عنه ، وجواز ما يجوز في حقه تعالى فرع عنه، فقال:
(۱) إنما قدم المصنّف الكلام في الإلهيَّات بوجه عام لأنَّها متعلقة بالله ، وما يتعلق به جل جلَّ شأنه مقدم على كل ما عداه ، وإنما بدأ من هذه المباحث الإلهية بيان ما يجب له سبحانه ؛ لأنَّ ذلك أشرفُ مباحث الإلهيات، وإنما قدم من مباحث الواجب له الكلام على الوجود ؛ لأن الوجود كالأصل لكل ما عداه ، وما عداه كالفرع له ، ألا ترى أنَّ الحكم بوجوب الواجبات له الله لا يتعقل إلا بعد الحكم بوجوب الوجود له. محي .
.
(۲) قوله: (بما هو الأصل) الأولى بالأدب أن يزيد الكاف إذ صفات الله تعالى لا يقال فيها أصل ولا فرع على سبيل الحقيقة كما لا جنس ولا فصل ولا عموم ولا خصوص خلافا لمن قال: أخص صفاته كذا وكذا متمسكا بأمور لا تفيد بل هو منفرد بجميع صفاته لا شبيه له فيها ولا شريك . الأمير .
The division of the topics of the art of tawhid into three sections. Therefore, he proceeded to detail what he summarized by saying first: 'Every legally accountable person is obligated to know...' the verse. And he began from the first section (1) with what is the origin (2), which is existence; because the judgment of the obligation of the obligatory [attributes] for Him, Most High, and the impossibility of what He is transcended from, and the permissibility of what is possible with respect to Him, Most High, are branches of it. So he said: (1) The author only presented speech about the divine matters in general because they pertain to Allah, and what pertains to Him, may His majesty be exalted, takes precedence over everything else. And he only began from these divine inquiries with the explanation of what is obligatory for Him, Blessed is He, because that is the noblest of the divine inquiries. And he only presented from the inquiries of what is obligatory for Him speech about existence because existence is like the origin for everything else, and everything else is like a branch for it. Do you not see that the judgment of the obligation of the obligatory [attributes] for Allah cannot be conceived except after the judgment of the obligation of existence for Him. – Muhyī. (2) His saying: 'with what is the origin' – the more proper [literary] approach is to add the kāf [of similarity], for the attributes of Allah, Most High, are not said to have an origin or a branch in a real sense, just as they have no genus, differentia, universality, or particularity, contrary to those who say: 'His most specific attribute is such and such,' clinging to things that are not beneficial. Rather, He is unique in all His attributes; He has no similar in them and no partner. – al-Amīr.
Source pages p. 209

Commentary
This passage is a commentary explaining why the author of the poem (likely Sanusi) began the topic of divine attributes with existence. It first states that the subjects of tawhid are divided into three sections. Then it notes that the author began detailing what he had summarized, quoting the first line of the poem: 'Every legally accountable person is obligated to know...' The commentary then explains that within the first section (divine matters), the author started with existence because it is the 'origin' (أصل) upon which all other judgments about Allah depend. The judgments of obligation (وجوب), impossibility (استحالة), and permissibility (جواز) regarding Allah's attributes are all branches (فرع) of existence. Two footnotes follow. The first (from Muhyī) elaborates on the order: the author prioritized speech about divine matters because they pertain to Allah, and within that, prioritized what is obligatory for Him because it is the noblest inquiry, and within that, prioritized existence because it is like the root for everything else. The second footnote (from al-Amīr) offers a literary caution: using the word 'origin' (أصل) for Allah's attributes should be understood metaphorically (with the particle 'ka' meaning 'like'), because in reality Allah's attributes have no origin, branch, genus, differentia, universality, or particularity. This refutes those who claim a most specific attribute for Allah. Allah is unique in all His attributes, having no similar or partner.

Key Terms
الوجود (existence)
الأصل (origin)
الفرع (branch)
الوجوب (obligation)
الاستحالة (impossibility)
الجواز (permissibility)
الإلهيات (divine matters)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Calling existence an 'origin' for Allah's attributes implies that Allah's attributes have a real composition or priority among themselves, similar to created things.
Correction: The commentator clarifies that 'origin' is used metaphorically. In reality, Allah's attributes have no origin, branch, genus, or differentia. He is unique in all His attributes.

Checkpoint
Why did the author begin with existence when discussing what is obligatory for Allah?
A) Because existence is the noblest of all attributes.
B) Because the judgment of all other obligatory, impossible, and permissible attributes depends on the judgment of existence.
C) Because existence is the most specific attribute of Allah.
D) Because the first line of the poem required it.

Key Takeaway
Existence is the foundational attribute in kalam because all other rational judgments about Allah depend on affirming His existence, but this must be understood as a conceptual priority, not a real one.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about your belief in Allah, do you truly start with the certainty of His existence as the foundation of all other attributes? Or do you sometimes assume His attributes without grounding them in that reality? Pause and ask yourself: Is my faith built on a firm conviction that Allah exists, or on mere habit? Commit today to renew your awareness of His existence before every act of worship.
?
The lesson warns against imagining a real hierarchy among Allah's attributes, even though we speak of existence as an 'origin'. Have you ever unconsciously ranked Allah's attributes—perhaps thinking mercy is more central than justice, or vice versa? How does this lesson correct your mental picture of Allah? Repent of any mental images that imply composition and resolve to affirm His absolute uniqueness in every attribute.
?
If all judgments about Allah branch from His existence, then what does that mean for your moments of doubt? When you struggle to believe in His mercy or justice, do you return to the foundational truth that He exists and is the source of all perfection? Write down one specific doubt you have faced and trace it back to a lack of certainty in His existence. Then recite and internalize the lesson's core idea: existence is the root.

Why List Qidam and Baqa' After Wujud? An Objection and Answer
Sanusi presents an objection that qidam and baqa' are redundant after wujud, and answers with a methodological justification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why classical theologians list pre-eternity (qidam) and everlastingness (baqa') as separate attributes alongside existence (wujud), even though necessary existence logically implies them both.

Core Idea
Theologians spell out implied attributes to prevent misunderstanding, not because they are unaware of the implication.

After establishing existence as the first necessary attribute, Sanusi anticipates a sharp objection: if necessary existence already implies pre-eternity and everlastingness, why waste ink listing them separately? The objection is not trivial — it touches on the methodology of theological writing. In this lesson, we’ll watch Sanusi concede the logical point but defend the practice on pedagogical grounds.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage once through. Notice how the objection is framed: it charges that listing qidam and baqa' after wujud is 'pure repetition' (takrār maḥḍ).
The objection is a common-sense argument about redundancy.
2
Analyze the author's concession and key terms
The author concedes that existence (wujud) entails qidam and baqa'. Identify the phrase 'dalalat al-iltizām' (implicative indication) and understand why it matters.
Dalalat al-iltizām means the implication where something necessarily follows from another.
3
Understand the pedagogical justification
The author explains that theologians do not rely on implication alone because ignorance in this field is severe. They explicitly state each attribute to prevent misunderstanding.
This is a methodological principle: clarity over conciseness when the stakes are high.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قلت: وجوب الوجود يستلزم القدم والبقاء جميعاً ، فذِكْرُ القِدَم والبقاء بعد ذكر الوجود يُعتبر تكرارًا محضاً ، فهلا صانَ العلماء كلامهم عن هذا التكرار المحض! فالجواب عن هذا: أنْ نُسلّم لكَ أنَّ ذِكرَ الوجودِ الذَّاتي يستلزم القدم والبقاء؛ لأنَّ ما بالذات لا يتخلَّفُ ، ولكنَّا نُنَبّهك إلى أنَّ علماء الكلام لا يكتفون بدلالة الالتزام، بل يُصرحون لشدّة خطر الجهل في هذا الفن، فلا يستغنون بملزوم عن لازم ولا بعام عن خاص.
If you say: Necessary existence entails both pre-eternity and everlastingness. So mentioning pre-eternity and everlastingness after mentioning existence is considered pure repetition. Why did the scholars not protect their speech from this pure repetition? The answer to this: that we concede to you that mentioning self-existence entails pre-eternity and everlastingness, because what is essential does not lag. But we draw your attention to the fact that the speculative theologians are not satisfied with the implication (dalalat al-iltizam), rather they explicitly state (the attributes) due to the severity of ignorance in this discipline. So they do not dispense with the necessary consequent in place of the primary implication, nor with the specific in place of the general.
Source pages p. 220

Commentary
This passage is an objection and answer regarding the seeming redundancy in the theological works that list existence (wujud), pre-eternity (qidam), and everlastingness (baqa') as separate attributes, when in fact necessary existence logically implies both qidam and baqa'. The objection claims this is repetition. The author answers by conceding that strictly speaking, existence does imply the others, but explains that theologians explicitly state them separately out of caution, because the implications may not be obvious to everyone. They do not rely solely on implication (iltizam) but make each explicit to prevent misunderstanding.

Key Terms
وجوب الوجود
القدم
البقاء
دلالة الالتزام
ملزوم
لازم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Listing qidam and baqa' after wujud is unnecessary repetition and shows carelessness by theologians.
Correction: The repetition is deliberate: theologians explicitly mention implied attributes to prevent ignorance and misunderstanding, especially in a field where precision is critical.

Checkpoint
Why do theologians mention qidam and baqa' separately despite wujud implying them?
A) Because wujud does not really imply them
B) To avoid dependence on implication and ensure clarity
C) Because they are more important
D) Because they are different types of wujud

Key Takeaway
While necessary existence logically implies pre-eternity and everlastingness, theologians explicitly list them out of caution to prevent ignorance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that because something is logically implied, you don't need to state it clearly—only to find that others misunderstood? How does this lesson challenge your own communication habits when it comes to important truths about Allah?
?
The theologians prioritized explicit mention over brevity due to the severity of ignorance. Where in your life have you prioritized convenience or assumed understanding over clear explanation, especially regarding matters of faith? Will you commit to being more explicit with your family or students about the implications of Allah's attributes?
?
If you truly believe that Allah's existence necessitates His pre-eternity and everlastingness, but you never articulate those attributes explicitly, how might that affect someone's understanding of Allah? Who in your circle needs to hear these attributes spelled out, not just implied?

Why Theologians Explicitly Mention Qidam and Baqa
Objection and answer about redundancy in listing attributes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Muslim theologians explicitly mention the attributes of preexistence and everlastingness after the necessity of existence, even though they are logically entailed.

Core Idea
Explicit mention prevents dangerous ignorance, even when implication suffices logically.

A sharp objection arises: if the necessity of existence already implies qidam and baqa, why do theologians repeat themselves? Sanusi does not dismiss the objection—he concedes the logical point. But then he reveals a deeper pedagogical principle: in matters of faith, clarity and prevention of error outweigh mere logical economy. This short exchange illustrates a key methodological rule in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
First, read the objection (from 'If you say...' to 'pure repetition'). Notice how the objector frames the issue as redundancy.
The objector assumes that logical entailment makes explicit mention unnecessary.
2
Understand the answer's structure
Read the answer. It begins with a concession ('We concede...'), then introduces the real reason: theologians are not satisfied with implication (dalāla al-iltizām) because of the severity of ignorance.
Look for the phrases 'دلالة الالتزام' and 'شدة خطر الجهل'.
3
Reflect on the methodological principle
The answer ends with a general rule: theologians do not rely on a malzūm to cover its lāzim, nor a general to cover a specific. Ask yourself why this rule matters in teaching theology.
This principle ensures every important attribute is explicitly taught.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قلت: وجوب الوجود يستلزم القدم والبقاء جميعاً، فذِكْرُ القِدَم والبقاء بعد ذكر الوجود يُعتبر تكراراً محضاً، فهلا صانَ العلماء كلامهم عن هذا التكرار المحض! فالجواب عن هذا: أنْ نُسلّم لكَ أنَّ ذِكرَ الوجودِ الذَّاتي يستلزم القدم والبقاء؛ لأنَّ ما بالذات لا يتخلَّفُ، ولكنَّا نُنَبّهك إلى أنَّ علماء الكلام لا يكتفون بدلالة الالتزام، بل يُصرحون لشدّة خطر الجهل في هذا الفن، فلا يستغنون بملزوم عن لازم ولا بعام عن خاص.
If you say: The necessity of existence entails both preexistence and everlastingness, so mentioning preexistence and everlastingness after mentioning existence is pure repetition. Why then did the scholars not protect their speech from this pure repetition? The answer to this is: We concede to you that mentioning essential existence entails preexistence and everlastingness, because what is essential does not fail to accompany. However, we draw your attention to the fact that the theologians (mutakallimun) are not satisfied with the implication (dalāla al-iltizām); rather, they state explicitly, due to the severity of the danger of ignorance in this art. Thus they do not dispense with a necessary concomitant in favor of that which necessitates it, nor with a specific matter in favor of a general one.
Source pages p. 220

Commentary
This passage is an objection and answer (su'āl wa jawāb). The objector argues that since the necessity of existence (wujūb al-wujūd) already entails both preexistence (qidam) and everlastingness (baqā'), explicitly listing these attributes after mentioning wujūd is pure redundancy. The answer grants the premise that essential existence indeed entails both, but explains that theologians deliberately avoid relying solely on implication (dalāla al-iltizām). Instead, they articulate attributes explicitly because the danger of ignorance in kalam is so great. They do not consider it sufficient to imply attributes from a more general term; they state each attribute directly to remove any ambiguity and to safeguard against misunderstanding. The final phrase emphasizes that they do not rely on a necessitating cause (malzūm) in place of its necessary concomitant (lāzim), nor a general term in place of a specific one.

Key Terms
وجوب الوجود
القدم
البقاء
دلالة الالتزام
ملزوم
لازم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Listing qidam and baqa after wujud is redundant and unnecessary.
Correction: While logically entailed, explicit mention is pedagogically crucial to prevent ignorance and error in matters of faith.

Checkpoint
Why do theologians explicitly mention qidam and baqa after wujud, according to the answer in this passage?
A) Because wujud does not actually entail them.
B) Because they want to increase the number of attributes.
C) Because they are not satisfied with implication due to the grave danger of ignorance.
D) Because they follow a strict rule of avoiding all repetition.

Key Takeaway
Theologians explicitly state logically entailed attributes because the risk of ignorance demands clarity over economy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever assume that because you understand a general principle about Allah, you don't need to reflect on its specific implications? How does the theologians' refusal to rely on implication challenge you to be more explicit in your own understanding and practice?

The scholars prioritized clarity over brevity because ignorance was too dangerous. In what area of your faith are you satisfied with vague or implied knowledge rather than seeking explicit certainty? What would it cost you to remain vague?
?
When you speak about Allah to others, do you rely on them to infer correct meanings, or do you state truths clearly and directly? What change will you make today to communicate as the theologians did—with explicit care?
